RATING: 10
Juegazo juegazo MAYUSCULO, de los mejores de gestión de recursos/acciones! Parece un eurogame pero tiene elementos de otros juegos. Muchísima variedad, bastantes cosas que recordar y muchas opciones en tu turno, pero todas ellas basadas en reglas sencillas e intuitivas. El arte gráfico muy bueno, 4 partidas cortas a 4, y 3 medias a 3 y 2, y otra larga a 3 jugadores, que me han dejado encantado y reafirmando que es un diseño de juego sublime! Una de ellas con semi-jugones. Escala bien, y la duración pues es muy variable, pues depende de que se cumpla uno de los objetivos de fin de partida. Comentan hasta versión en solitario (que nunca me ha atraído) que esta bien. Obra maestra sin duda alguna entre los juegos de mesa.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I was very interested in trying Archipelago since it caused so much exploding rage and bursts of anger at the boardgame club previous week. Personally I was not part of that game but the repercussions were felt at our table as well. It was hard to believe that a game could be as bad as it sounded.
 
Now that I have played I can say that it is not bad. It is just boring with a number of annoying elements.
 
First issue is with the fragmented rules and mechanics. There are so many things you can do that all are relatively simple as themselves, but since they very loosely tied together, you are facing a mess that takes quite a lot of time to explain. Moreover, all paths you can take are very short which results in a game in which you do a little bit of everything. I reckon, this works well with the hidden scoring and game ending conditions but does not really give you a feeling of actually accomplishing something. If you play with the scoring cards openly visible to everyone, the game is even more boring. You are just repeating the two to three actions that allow you to take very small incremental steps without ever achieving anything bigger. To add to the misery the game is so low scoring that you are absolutely disappointed. You play for 2-3 hours and get, what, 7 victory points? It might be just me but I do not feel satisfied.
 
Second issue is with the playability. I absolutely detest games in which you have to be craning over the table in order to read that minuscule text on your opponents' cards or the cards on the display. This creates a lot of downtime since everyone will be doing it on their turn. Furthermore, the board looks quite busy and it is really hard to tell the building tiles from each other without closer inspection. I do not have bad eyes but still I struggled with gathering all information I needed.
 
Third issue is with the cooperative aspect of the game. I think it is supposed to work as a catch up mechamism, but since you cannot easily tell who is winning there is no real incentive for you to do anything because you do not know whether you are actually helping yourself or the others. Thus it comes to the responsibility of players last in turn order to decide whether to continue the game or have everyone lose. This works against my concept of meaningful decisions. I can think of an easy fix, though. The rules could enforce cooperation, i.e., you have to help if you can and thus would be paying for being allowed to play first. But I am not sure how this would change the experience.
 
There are some positives as well. Exploration and tile laying is fun. And you can choose to push your luck, which is usually exciting. However, in Archipelago exploration can be extremely frustrating as well because there is a big chance that the tile simply does not fit anywhere which results in an action lost with the game stalling. In my opinion this is a very dangerous avenue in game design. There is a high risk of losing potential fans if they are too well smitten by chance on their first plays. Moreover, I feel that the game should progress with every move and on every turn. Too much stalling just makes you lose interest.
 
I can see that Archipelago requires a new way of thinking and traditional approaches to eurogames are not appropriate here. Personally I am fine with this and I am very flexible with my playing style and can adapt to most games. But if you have to tell everyone at the table how the game should be played for it to be fun, something is terribly wrong.

RATING: 6
A good game with a lot of theme. I was surprised at how much I felt like a colonial governor and the myriad of problems of running a new colony, with new world and old world problems. The pieces are beautiful and there is negotiations and player interaction that is most satisfying. 

Downside is it takes a while to play. The short game variant that is too short and does not give the game mechanics justice in my opinion. And some people might not like the backstabbing or negotiations that can be done in the game. And also if someone finds themselves trailing a lot, they can sabotage the game by causing the revolt in which all loses but that is group specific. 
 
Overall, I personally enjoyed it a lot and the theme is really immense and makes it a winner in my view.

RATING: 9
FAVOURITE GAME - One of my favourite euros and byfar the one with the nicest design. It combines tile placement with worker placement and a bit of bidding. Basically you have to explore, build up, and then manage an island together with your fellow players. Then you have to trade goods to receive the almighty gold to be able to expand. A nice edge in this game is that you never know when it will end, since at the beginning everyone has a secret objective which once accomplished triggers the end of the game. Besides, it has a short, medium term and long playing version so it can accomodate novices and hardcore europlayers too.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
One of the worst games of all time.  The semi-coop in this game is completely and utterly broken, as there is no reward for attempting to prevent the group-loss condition, and in fact, making any attempt to prevent it will result in opponents who are not trying to prevent it becoming massively, uncontestably ahead of you.

Additionally, turn order bidding is outrageously broken, with the winner of a blind bid determining play order for ALL players, and being allowed to then take bribes from these players, while the other bidders actually lose their bids to get nothing in return.  So lets say one player bids '5' and another bids '4'.  The player who bids 5 goes first, can actually gain resources back from the others by accepting bribes, making him net pay LESS than the '4' bidder, and then can shove the '4' bidder into last position.  The '4' bidder loses his money.

When a "game" breaks uncontrollably when played by players who understand games and try to win, then it is a failure that should not have made it through playtesting, and its developers and playtesters have failed in their duty to present a playable game to the public.

RATING: 6
There's parts of this game that I like a heck of a lot, and then there's parts that outright annoy me and drag the whole thing down. It's a mixture of shocking highs and lows to my own personal enjoyment.

I really, really like everything about the tiles, exploration, gathering resources, constructing buildings, variable VP goals, the markets, and all that good stuff. 

Conversely, the turn order mechanic is bleh. I've never liked a single semi co-op element in any game I've played so far (including here). The end game mechanic can be very annoying when it's suddenly over and you don't even have a chance to finish the round. And perhaps most egregiously, I find myself uninterested in the type of gameplay required to utilize the cards and negotiation aspects well at all.

There's a whole game of manipulating the card market, watching what people buy, and buying the many nasty screw each other cards that counter what everyone else is buying/doing. You can also use many of the cards other people purchase, opening up lots of room for even more surprising play with them. That, combined with all of the potential political maneuvering and bribing leaves a wide field of choices to play a game I'm not all that interested in playing. Negotiation is purely un-structured--allowing virtually anything. I personally find freedom in boundaries, whereas I detect the potential for madness with all these options.

Maybe the aspects I dislike aren't as bad as I think and repeated play would get me used to them. But the nastiness of cards and grubby politicking that this game asks for is not my style, and I feel like it impedes my enjoyment of the rest of the fantastic mechanics. So overall I'd currently rate my experience as just decent.

RATING: 6
Explore a new world and exploit its resources, though not too much or the natives will revolt and we'll all lose the game.  Now that's quite a neat idea.  Unfortunately, Boelinger burdens that concept with a whole bunch of sub-systems. Even his bells and whistles have bells and whistles.

The idea of offering different game lengths by modifying the game end conditions is another neat one.  Just be aware than game length can remain quite variable and that the short game is prone to fizzling out just when you're getting a handle on all those fiddly mechanics.  

The artwork is quite pretty, though it is ridiculously difficult to distinguish native huts on the land tiles.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3.5
Archipelago is probably the most beautiful board game I have ever seen and this is one of the reasons it gets a high rating. The game play is engaging and for those who like secret objective and mind games it might be an awesome experience. I must confess that I prefer the Euro-games with no hidden information, but Archipelago was still a pleasant experience. The rules are a bit heavy for the this kind of game and I had a hard time explaining it while the game play flows smoothly but ends abruptly when a player reveals that one of his end of game conditions was met. 

Overall I am not profoundly impressed by Archipelago, but the quality of the components, the replay value and the awesome graphics are adding together to a game that I will for sure play again.

EDIT: after 2 more plays, rating decreased to 6. Nothing more than beautiful components.

EDIT: nothing more than beauty. I am considering re-writing the victory conditions and the whole card mechanic from scratch using existing components just to use the very nice graphics.

RATING: 7
This is a gamers' game with a cornucopia of mechanisms, perhaps too many, all thrown into a reasonably thematic game.  The first time play is rife with trouble as there is just so much going on.  

It feels like perhaps this game should have shipped with rules describing a base game, then an intermediate game where additional mechanics are added, then an advanced game with all the mechanics. Too many things in this game as far as I am concerned.  I particularly dislike the rebellion mechanic and the iconography and symbology used in the rebellion mechanic.

Still, there are some interesting decisions in this game once you get past the tangled thicket of things that are going on that you frankly just don't like or care for...so you ignore them...undoubtedly at your peril...and possibly...eventual major league annoyance...like the rebellion mechanic.

RATING: 5.8
Ambitious, as close to a "sandbox" game as you'll find. While the concept is interesting, the game itself feels half-baked, lots of ideas that aren't executed well enough to make the play as tense or interesting as it should be. A few of the mechanisms are just terrible, like the auction for turn order where you lose your money regardless of whether you win or lose the auction.

I'm not against the idea of a semi-coop, but I think it's poorly done here. Many of the actions you can take to stop the rebellion have no benefit to you, they only help other people. This is always a bad idea in a mechanic.

RATING: N/A
One play with a friends copy.  Beautiful game, good production quality and a nice amount of tension to be had.

First: we're here to help the islands prosper
Second: We're here to make money and fulfill our own secret goals
Third: we're here to make more money than our competitors while completing our secret agendas before they do.
Lastly: we're here to do all the above while keeping the natives happy enough not drive all the non natives out of town.

So if juggling those political axes while settling a vibrant island chain sounds up your alley, archipelago may be right for you.

RATING: 8
A unique semi-cooperative, civilization game that needs the right people to make it fun. I love how it incorporates many euro mechanisms while keeping a strong thematic feel. Plenty of options you can take as you try to build a strong economic engine while working towards your secret objectives and working with the other players to avoid an uprising. Absolutely beautiful artwork.

I'm not sure how I feel about certain aspects. Negotiation, while it adds a lot of player interaction and another layer of decision making, lacks structure since you can negotiate about anything at any time. There's also not enough incentive to give up your own valuable resources if you can get the other players to do so.

RATING: 7
1/28/2015:  There's a solo expansion out that's apparently pretty good.

12/1/2012: I'm definitely a fan of longer civ building games.  I definitely like this one and prefer it over Sid Meier's Civ.  It has a nice tech tree, and a variety of raw materials.

I was surprised to find that resource scarity is a factor in this game.  Our first short game with 5 took 4 hours and I'm looking forward to another play with fewer players.

To me it seemed a little spongy.  I was thinking that it could be tightened up a bit by reducing the number of choices.  One one hand it feels a little long, on the other you really don't get as far as you want before the game ends.  I do agree that there can be swings in the victory points based on the hidden objectives, but that seems to bother me less than most.

Definitely prefer over Sid Meier's Civ.  Need another play to compare it to Through the Ages.

RATING: 5
Rég volt ennyire felemás érzésem egy játék után... A sok szép alkatrész és az érdekes a szabály ismeretében úgy álltam neki, hogy nagy élmény lesz. A téma is megfogott, és tetszett az is, hogy nagy szabadságot ad a játék, kevés szűkös akció van. Kifejezetten izgalmasnak tűnt az előkészületek alapján. És maga a mechanizmus is kedvemre való, néhány ötletes újítást is találtam: a kártyák árának változása pl. nagyon elegánsan van megoldva, a licit is elég egyedi. Látszólag minden a játék mellett szól.
De: néhány kör után rájöttem, hogy a játék hosszára vonatkozó bizonytalanság nekem nagyon nem tetszik. Nem tudhatom, hogy 1 óra múlva lesz-e vége, vagy 5 óra múlva (más ilyen játék is van, Düne pl., de ott valamiért tolerálhatóbb ez). Az egyes végefeltételek bekövetkezési valószínűsége közt nagyságrendi különbségek vannak. Persze jó a titokzatosság, de nekem ez így tervezhetetlen. A játék igazolta is a félelmeimet, mert egyik akcióm közben a játékostársam váratlanul bejelentette, hogy MOST vége... Túl komplex ahhoz a játék, hogy ilyen lutri vége legyen szerintem. Ezt max egy fél órás játéknál tolerálom, vagy esetleg más műfajú játéknál. A másik ami zavart, hogy a kollektív vereség pár körön belül megakadályozhatatlanul bekövetkezhet, ha valaki arra hajt, és mivel van egy célkártya, ami erre ösztönöz, ezért teszteletlenségre gyanakszom (ezt a kártyát nem ajánlatos belekeverni az elején, mert megölné a játékot). A kollektív vereség réme egyébként akár még érdekes is lehet, de mivel nehéz azonos terhet vállalni a veszély elhárításából, kérdéses, mennyire igazságos utána győztest hirdetni. További hiba, vagy inkább megint csak teszteletlenség, hogy az alacsony végpontszámok (8-10 pont) ellenére nevetségesen apró dolgokon fordulhat meg akár 3-4 pont sorsa is... Nem tudom, hogy a titkos célkártyák, vagy a fél-coop jelleg öli-e meg a játékot, de valamiért nem az igazi. Komoly játékhoz képest túl sok benne a lutri, komolytalan játéknak meg túl hosszú és összetett (közel 2 óra volt a szabály). Ha egy mondatban kellene a játék lényegét összefoglalni: Gyönyörű és ígéretes játék, de nem vagyok benne biztos, hogy tudatosan több pontot érek el, mint totál random lépésekkel:)
Érdekesség: nálunk az nyert, akinek messze a legkevesebb embere volt.

RATING: 7.5
Archipelago is at the same time an ingenious work of art and an utterly broken piece of design.

The semi-cooperative part is what drives my excitement for the game, and the main problem as well: with little to no incentive to prevent the common loss for the trailing players, it can easily fall apart (and it almost did in my first 5-players game) if the players don't embrace the design philosophy behind it.

Behind this great and crazy idea, there is also a brilliant design: action selection (á la worker placement but not quite), exploration, resource and market management... Everything falls in place to create an epic game, with a rich narrative and a great story arc.

There is lots of replayability, guaranteed (beyond the exploration tiles) by the trend and hidden objective cards, as well as the three different durations (even if I wouldn't bother playing Archipelago in the short version: there is no time to guess other players' objectives).

The only thing I really don't like is the blind bidding for turn order, with the winner determining all the positions.

In the end, I'd like to play it more to form a coherent and solid opinion about Archipelago. After just three plays, the tentative rating is 7.5, but could really go up or down based on its mastepiecery or brokenness... Which one will prevail?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
The semi co-op just didn't work for us. At first it was surprisingly difficult to explain, and after the game ended we didn't feel like we have won together and Person A performed best. All but Person A felt like having lost the game.

Furthermore there a bunch o things I consider un-elegant and not intuitive:
- workers sometimes have to be lied sideways and put up again, just to lie them sideways again in another phase
- the double sided improvement/crisis cards, where some crisis trigger immediately but others just in certain phases
- buying an improvement becomes a great risk because you at the same time reveal a new crisis card (this makes the game very punishing)
- the losing player can tank the game and force a all-lose scenario...


But it looks gorgeous after the game has ended and the archipelago has been explored.

RATING: 8
Archipelago is a pretty fascinating game. There's an interesting marketplace mechanism, exploration of beautifully illustrated tiles, and a need to balance every action against the native population's tolerance for your colonial meddling. The best part, however, is trying to work out the secret victory conditions from the actions of everyone else around the table, and then trying to figure out if one player is actually working against the group and trying to incite rebellion. How to work around the Separatist, balancing your need to score points against the need to keep the game alive, is perhaps the trickiest part of the game to get to grips with, and after several games I must admit I still haven't got a handle on it. I keep wanting to come back to it, though, so it must be doing something right!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I like this game a lot but I've had mixed experiences with the people I played it with. Some loved it while others didn't like it. 

To me this game is a bit of a godsent because it has a lot of those 4X mechanics that I love, and at the same time it plays in under two hours which makes it easier to get on the table.

Another thing that I like about it is that every game feels very different because of the various evolution cards. One game might be a compact little archipelago with plenty of war and tension, while the next game might be a huge series of islands where everybody has plenty of land on which they can live and grow peacefully.

My advice: If you want a game of strategy instead of luck then use the Open Goals variant from the rulebook. That way everybody know what to go for. Otherwise you fulfill your secret mission and then you'll have to guess what the other player's missions are.

RATING: 8
One of the very first games I owned. Way back before I started to register game plays. And it's still my favorites. The art, the exploration part, the semi-coop part, the possibility of a 'traitor', the whole design it's all right up my alley. 
Although after some plays and gaming experience I must admit this game has flaws. Mainly it concerns private objective cards. The end game conditions are not at all balanced. Even for a short game they vary very much. Getting to 30 citizens for 4 player game is way faster than building 6 ports. It means that in the game without some conditions the short game could last 2h longer than with them. 
The other thing is that private scorings differ too much. Holding to exploration tokens is way easier than building a lot of ports. So the player with exploration objective has a big advantage if he manages to not give that away.
The last thing is the coop part of the game. It can stir the emotions. If there is a crisis that has to be prevented sometimes someone has to give more than the rest. And if later he feels that the rest is not giving that much to the common cause it can be frustrating. And you have to remember that this is a semi-coop so first and foremost everyone is being selfish. It's not necessarily a drawback but something worth keeping in mind.

RATING: 8
I'll need more plays of this to see how sustainable it is.  It may just be that I score this game a point higher than I might simply because I love the theme so much.  

I'm neutral to the hidden aspects of the game, though I do like the tension that develops as players try and guess what their opponents are doing.  In the way it's designed, playing without hidden objectives (i.e. having all objectives visible to all players) would make the scoring very uninteresting, as one player could easily dominate.  The hidden objectives encourage players to do a bit of everything.  However, this shines most in a four player game.

The thing I like least is the bidding system, where the loser of the bid loses the money.  Not a huge fan of that mechanic.

RATING: 6
18-Nov-12: Initial thoughts after 2 plays (1 short 2 player, 1 medium 4-player that ended in revolution).  

This is probably my favorite macro civ game (which isn't saying too much).  It removes conflict and replaces it with negotiation and hidden objectives.  It also adds in a significant market aspect while removing the tech tree.  This makes it unlike any other macro civ game I've played.  Of all of the recent games I've played, this one has the steepest learning curve, not just for one player but for the entire group.  After a couple of plays, I think the market is very important.  Having goods gives money for buying cards and people and having goods allow players to prevent revolution from ending the game.  On the other hand, buildings are also  likely to be winning conditions (i.e., VPs) so ignoring them is risky.  

I think the short game is only good as a teaching game.  The medium game is probably fine with higher player counts, but with 2, I think the long game is necessary to ensure adequate player interaction.  Negotiation is necessary but I don't think this game suffers too much from a "gang up on the leader" issue (besides the blow up the world option -- see below).  

It feels like a game where players play the other players as much as they do the game.  That said, with the randomized hexes, cards, and objectives, there should be a good amount of replayability.  I may prefer to play with open objectives, which will maintain the variability but decrease some of the uncertainty (not a bad thing, but I'm not sure I like it in a 2+ hour game).  

One issue with the game is the "blow up the world" option.  If a couple of players are convinced they're losing, it is relatively easy for them to either cause everyone to lose or force the leaders to shoulder the burden of continuing the game and give those that are behind a chance to catch up (of course, if they don't say anything until they decide to blow up the world, the leaders may have no chance to stop them).  

The rulebook also has some issues (i.e., situations not addressed) but I expect in time BGG will answer these problems.  

I am not sure how often I'll get to play it, but I did enjoy my couple of plays.  I may end up keeping it as my one macro civ game.  I'm still not sure and will need more plays to decide.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Juego de gestionar a unos trabajadores en un archipielado, para ir consiguiendo recursos y con ellos construir edificios e ir acercándote a tus objetivos. Cada jugador tiene un objetivo secreto (que puntuará para todos), con lo que tienes que andar con mil ojos a ver qué hacen los demás.

Además, es semicolaborativo. Todos los jugadores deben velar porque los habitantes no se rebelen, porque en tal caso pierden todos los jugadores.

Está muy bien pensado y los parámetros del juego se relacionan unos con otros de maneras indirectas que hacen que sea complicado preveer las consecuencias de tus acciones, en conjunción con las de los demás jugadores.
Orientado a jugones, sin duda. Dificil de explicar y complicado de dominar,pero muy absorvente y divertido.

RATING: 7
A really interesting blend of mechanics. Area control using modular maps, resource management, complex economic choices within both domestic and foreign markets, plus the unique rebel and unemployment tracks make this game unlike any I have ever played. I really like the game play, the beautiful components, and the theme.

With that said, however, why oh why did they make the rebels black, represented by a black pawn, and offensively portrayed as primitive "barbarians" sticking their tongues out? It's just horrible, far worse than the slaves in Puerto Rico, and almost ruins what is otherwise a really good game. I will never understand why that artwork was approved and am personally considering pasting a different picture over every card and board in my own copy (or just blacking it out with a marker).

As others have mentioned, the semi-coop element does allow those in last place to hold the game hostage.  However, it is up to the leaders to make sure they don't spend too many of their resources and be prepared to take the brunt of responsibility for crises as they come. Negotiation is key, and it should be house ruled that no one deliberately throw the game unless they have no other choice (or are the Separatist).

RATING: 7
A lot of familiar mechanics, but together in a really neat system.  You've got events that make each round painful on the players, you've got some worker placement, you've got some economics, you've got some resource management, etc.  Lets of stuff, but it all comes together pretty logically.

The high points for me:

- Each player only knows of one possible end game condition, so there is always pressure to get things done since you're never sure when the game might suddenly end!

- The game can end with nobody winning if the archipelago revolts, so there is a small amount of cooperative game here.  If the players don't all work together against the game a little bit, everyone loses.

- A pretty neat economic system, and I generally really enjoy economic games.

Overall, just a really neat game that feels pretty fresh despite using a lot of familiar mechanics.

Also, a side note: I don't know what the people recommending this for two players are smoking.  There is negotiating in this game, and there is also a battle over points for doing various things.  Neither is exciting for two.

RATING: 4
Feel I need a few more plays to figure out how I feel about this game. The gameplay is interesting, with clever and varied play required. The scoring is truly odd, and I can't tell if I like it or not. You really do need to pay attention to others' actions to get in on their scoring. The natives aspect feels pretty dumb. Makes absolutely no sense to contribute valuable resources to defeat them. Definitely enjoyed it though. 

Update: Dropping rating from 7 to 4. The game has a lot of interesting things going on, but ultimately fails. The fact that there is no compensation for contributing resources to defeat the natives essentially means you should never do it. So a huge part of the game - the cooperative part - just doesn't work. And that makes the 'traitor' role way too powerful. Way too much luck in what tiles come out, and what you choose, vis a vis the end game scoring conditions. Plus, the rules are just massively convoluted and even after several plays tough to get your head around and remember everything. It's close and it's definitely not boring, but I'm happy to never play this again. Ever. I'll leave it at 4 since my 3 plays were at least interesting.

RATING: 9
Fantastic game.  Extremely competitive.  Lots of player interaction via dialogue rather than direct confrontation on the board (although it can show up via passive aggressive building and resource gathering).  Although possible with 2 players, you really need 3 or more to get a good above the board game going, which is where this game really shines.

The learning curve is huge!  Plan on taking at least an hour to cover all the rules for your first game, and then spend that first game putting around (the game does offer 3 tiers of length by means of end game objectives.  Very cool!).  After your group gets through this initial struggle, however, you will be rewarded with a game that has a huge amount of variety in terms of choices and strategy.

The art is fantastic!  EVERY SINGLE TILE has unique art, and the illustration on the cards is great also.  The components are good for their tasks.  The island tiles though, as stated early, are truly fantastic and make this game a joy to watch unfold on your table.

I would love to give this game a 10, however, the rules are so dense it can take a few times playing through before you're actually playing the game properly.  Complex game design is not something to bring a game down a notch, I know, but the rule book doesn't do a good enough job of easing you into the experience and answering those nit-picky questions.  With that said, this game does so much right!  Economy simulation, meta gaming, great art, real player to player interaction, resource manipulation.  So much good going on here!

RATING: 9
Subversive is definitely the right word for this game.  Archipelago neatly inverts euro-paradigms by loading the game with genre cliches in the form of cruel optical illusions.  New players will grope for the walls and fumble for the corners.  Then, in a panic-induced insight, they will make for the exit, only to slam into a pane of glass.  It's quite the carnival fun-house.  

Weirdly, it's not dissimilar from the kinds of conventional genre deconstruction in Keyflower, only here the process stays in a deconstructive mode well through play.  The game is highly unstable, both in the fragile "balance-beam" manner similar to The Great Zimbabwe and in the sense that the game itself is in a constant state of flux.

Here I'm tempted to bring in games like Pax Porfiriana (and Flux, for that manner) but I think that, mechanically speaking, that there is something very sophisticated going on.  While the game is constantly changing there is far more stability in the player positions.  It's less of a roller coaster and more a torrential downpour where the players get to route the water through that same hall of mirrors.  

All of this says nothing about the negotiations that form the heart of the game.  In the first game with my wife we scarcely traded--merely out of habit.  After we got used to the game we found ourselves cutting deals left-and-right.  It's remarkable how much interesting negotiation this game supports even in a two player setting.

So, in summary.  The game is good.  And, what's more, it's a good game that has ambition to match.  It's been a long time since I encountered a designer a game of such startling scope.  There is something almost Eklund-esque about it, but here Eklund's pedagogical charge has been replaced by an almost sadistic mechanical playfulness.  If the design's craftsmanship can match its ambition the game will hold its rating.  Even if it doesn't, it's failure and fall should prove more interesting than most designs.

RATING: 10
I'm giving this an initial rating of an 8 but I could easily see this rating going up to 9 or 10. I really enjoyed the combination of meta-gaming & tactical gaming that was present in this game.

I would say that is MUST be played with the right group. A lot of people will struggle with the concept that everyone can lose so you have to work together but you are actually competing the entire time.

The dynamic of having no idea when the game will end and having to guess at what other victory conditions are out there is a brilliant stroke.

Probably the most unique game I can remember playing.

RATING: 8
Archipelago is as hard to pronounce as it is to play. 
With that i mean it is a hard game but yet very fun. 

The game is that you and your friends are playing explorers that finds the new world and semi-cooperative in a very polite way exploit the natives resources and sometime enslave them.
The game never tells you that you do that but you are kinda feeling that you do when you pay a one time payment for a human to work on a pineapple field for 50 years.

In a round it goes like this: 
1. Reset all the meeples
2. Out bid your friends and then blackmail them for play order. 
3. Move meeples and cubes that are a track and see how the doom creeps up on you.
4. Everybody rebels and wont work so they lay down in the mud, this happens the same time you need to handle a crisis or two, and then maybe.. just maybe.. some meeples will stand up and work. 
5. The game becomes a worker placer game. 

Love this game to bits, it's hard and great and awesome and your friend wont like you but you will have the most points but then everybody dies.... 

Must buy

RATING: 5.4
There are just too many things being crammed into this game for it to work, in my opinion. Just getting through the rules took an unreasonably long time.

In particular the aspect of the game that didn't really work for me was the negotiation aspect, where players are choosing to contribute to the common good in order to avoid penalties. This on top of keeping track of two markets, where you can/can't place your workers, and what tiles can be placed where...it was just too much.

Now on the plus side, I will say the art here is well done, and I did really like that you can control for the length of the game. Overall, though - this is not my cup of tea.

RATING: 4
The designer bills this game as "semi-cooperative."  I'll just bill it as "weird."  It's a eurogame, but its rule have an Ameritrashy inelegance to them that overshadowed my first play.  For example, some units can be "engaged," but can nonetheless reproduce or recruit?

What's most concerning was that I kept asking myself what I should be doing:  What is the export market for?  How do ports/markets work again?  Turn order determination is completely broken, and there's not much benefit to turn order anyway.  

The "semi-cooperative" element is a good-faith attempt to inject some novelty and excitement into an otherwise hardcore euro framework, but I think it falls flat because there's nothing to prevent players from ignoring opportunities to bargain.  Much like Dixit, if others don't play along, the game is missing part of its allure.  

Overall, this game is just unpolished like its spiritual predecessor Civilization: The Board Game.  Too long, too weird, too boring.  In the end, it feels like I'm playing a prototype.  Case in point, there's an egregious misprint in the migration section.  In the first paragraph, OR should be AND/OR.    
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A few years ago the weight of this game would have thrown me. Now? It's middle weight. But since there is so much to do and remember in the game, how can that be? The THEME! It holds it all together for me. Easy to remember the rules and strategies easily present themselves.

Played this a few times, and while it is fun when you play along with the theme, it suffers when you focus on winning and then reveal to yourself some of the weaknesses of the how the game works. Initiative (the first player) and the domestic and foreign market crisis weigh heavily toward that first player- as do a lot of other aspects of the game. There is a subtle runaway aspect to the game that rewards success so that if you carefully construct your game, you can usually walk away with a win.

For a game this complex, it was a surprising discovery on my part- I usually can't dominate games as well as I can this one. So the challenge for me is a little lost.

However, I like the game and am happy with owning it!

RATING: N/A
$55 BGG
Players are Renaissance European powers competing in the exploration of a Pacific or Caribbean archipelago. They will explore territories, harvest resources, use those resources in markets both internal (for their use and that of the natives) and foreign (to sell it in Europe), build markets, harbors, cities and temples, and negotiate among themselves (and maybe betray each other) – all this to complete their secret objectives. They will also need to guess the secret objective of the other players to be able to benefit from them. But players also need to be careful of the natives; if they make them too unhappy or if too many of them are unoccupied, they could revolt and declare independence. Then everyone will lose!

RATING: 6
I have dropped my rating (a few times, now) all the way down to 6 (from an initial 9!).

EDIT: We've done a lot of work on a variant for this game (since we do love so much about it). For our groups, this variant brings the game back up to at least an 8: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/933938/a-more-moderate-archipelago-variant

Even though the game is definitely fun and entertaining, the end game conditions and scoring are a huge letdown. Generally, you just barely have time to start to deduce what the secret scoring objectives will be--and then the game abruptly ends because one of the end game conditions was triggered.

You could treat the game as a pure co-op, but the co-op element isn't really very challenging unless the majority of the players are simply ignoring rebellion. We've yet to be in any real danger of the rebels winning.

Lastly, the game is very low-scoring. Most likely, you'll get around 10-15 points total. Especially with 5 players, this means that the game frequently ends in a tie... and the tiebreaker is money. Therefore, whichever player just did a cash-generating action is likely to beat the player who just took a cash-spending action.

We do love playing the game, but the outcome is so uncontrollable and random that it's disappointing.

We are actively trying to come up with some better scoring possibilities (perhaps including scoring for things done during gameplay) and end game conditions (perhaps having several victory conditions like in Civilization The Board Game).

We have played long and short games with both 2 and 5 players. The longest game, with two players, seems to mitigate these problems the best.

RATING: 9
An ambitious work of board gaming art.  Archipelago is an impressive and unique tapestry of mechanics that somehow manages to both coalesce seamlessly together and perfectly reflect its theme.  It is quite a long list of mechanics: bidding, worker placement, drafting, tableau building, tile placement, hidden roles/objectives, resource production/conversion, trading/negotiation, variable market...  all wrapped together in a semi-coop 3X game.

All of the core "solitaire" gameplay in Archipelago is smooth and compelling on its own.  Exploring new tiles, expanding one's colony across the map, and exploiting those areas for resources is a satisfying puzzle to optimize.  However, those elements are all pretty standard-euro.  It is the interactive elements in Archipelago that set it apart.  The levels of interaction are staggering and direct, from bidding for turn order, to the hidden end-game triggers and scoring, to being able to use everyone else's evolution cards.  The impact of these interactions are intensely magnified by the semi-coop goal of avoiding native unrest and the simple fact that any deal can be made with any resources/promises at any time.  Archipelago may not have the "extermination" required to be categorized as a 4X, but it instead replaces that 4th X with a more mature (and sometimes more brutal) cacophony of interaction.

The weak point of Archipelago is the end-game.  This is also what makes the game somewhat fragile and divisive.  Both the hidden scoring and the existence of a traitor card can be justified as necessary.  Without hidden objectives, it would be easy to determine which player has the lead, and then the semi-coop and trading systems would completely fall apart.  That said, the hidden objectives make the end-game somewhat unsatisfying as the worlds of social-deduction games and euro games collide.

The components and artwork of Archipelago are excellent.  The artwork on the hex tiles is particularly beautiful, adding significant table appeal and enhancing the thematic experience.

RATING: 6
I finally got a game in last week and we had a good time playing it. Here are my thoughts:

Some aspects of the game are clunky. For instance, constructing a ship does not tie up a worker although constructing anything else does. It's a weird exception that's hard to remember. Reproduction does not tie up a worker, Recruitment does not tie up a worker... most other actions do tie up workers. A consistent rule on this would make it easier to teach the rules to new players, and easier to play.

The hidden game end and scoring is interesting, but it can lead to significant variation in the game play length. In our game, none of our hidden cards had game endings that were coming up, so the game took longer than it would have if some of the shorter game options came up. This might be designed to keep people from throwing the co-op side of the game, since there is so much uncertainty about the objective and the when the game will end, but I'm not sure if it works as a solution, since the knife can cut both directions (in other words, the person winning will not know he is winning, and might decide not to save the game only because he thinks he might be losing). In my case, I need consistent playing times so I can let me wife know when I'll be back to take care of my son, so variable playing times of this range is problematic.

The bidding for turn order is a very harsh mechanic. I won the first bid with 5 coins. I bartered to get back 5 coins from other players who wanted to play earlier in the turn order, a resource (I forget what it was), and I still ended up going first that round. The person who bid 4 coins was hurt pretty badly. This mechanics will lead to some huge swings, which would be fine if the person winning had to over compensate giving resources to "the greater good", but it's hard to know who is winning towards the end of the game, which makes it hard to be magnanimous. In this case, it was the first turn, I was clearly winning, so I took on a bigger portion of the burden. But by the end of the game I had no idea who was winning, and was much more reluctant to hand over a win though my magnanimousness. In the end, I was surprised to learn that I won by a very large margin. 

I can't gauge whether the hidden role of the Separatist will fix the problem of players throwing the game if they think they are losing. I am skeptical, though in our game, there was a Separatist, and we did not have a rebellion, so who knows...

There is a lot going on in the game, so it's hard to get a good measure with only one play. The four of us enjoyed the game, but we were all lukewarm about wanting to play again (possible in light of the fact we all want to play A Game of Thrones again, and our time for long games is very limited (several of us have young kids)). I like that the game is experimental. The concepts are cool. The implementation is quite good. But I think there are too many aspects that are a slightly clunky for Archipelago to stick around as a staple. I might be wrong though. It all depends on whether a 10% chance of any given player having the Separatist card and the opacity of whether you are doing well, is enough to counter balance partial co-op aspect of the game. If so, then I think most people will be willing to over-look the other clunky rule exceptions. 

So that's my two cents worth. I look forward to reading more comments from others, and hopefully playing again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I'm going to give the game the benefit of the doubt and rate it an 8 after just one (all too) short game.

Easier to play than you'd have guessed and with all the randomness in setup and card draws the game will probably not get old quick. Feels like there are many different routes to victory as well.

RATING: 5
Wow, this is a hard one to rate.  Part of me wants to really like this, buy it, and explore the gameplay.  But the other side of me finds some serious flaws in this game. Ultimately, there is so much potential in this game, but it barely misses the mark.

It has a nice theme to it.  It presents you with good decisions to make.

But the negatives are: Long game play (we played medium game).  Pointless negotiations.  Too much bickering over a dollar.  Reminded me of Wealth of Nations in that regard.  Difficulty in determining end of game conditions.  There are many and the board gets too cluttered to make the determination easily without giving things away.  An "out of contention" player will likely to try to end the game.  That player was me. I was not successful in ending it because it ended before I could rouse the rebels.  Much to my surprise I almost won the game!  So really it is hard to know how well you are doing.  Given this, what should a player actually be doing to win?

RATING: 5
Players represent colonial powers, exploring an untapped Archipelago (loosely based on the South Pacific), and exploiting it for wealth.

Lavishly produced game, choc full of lovely components.  Players work through a busy sequence of play, taking actions from a large selection of choices, to explore, reproduce, exploit, trade, build, produce, tax, and... you get the idea.  Layered on top is a double market system, where goods are bought and sold domestically as well as internationally.  Layered on top of this (!) is the local population, who want to be employed, happy, and want for nothing, and will lurch into rebellion if players are not careful, ensuring that the game ends with nobody winning. 

Busy game, feels very French, complete with loose rules, and a kitchen sink design philosophy.  On top of everything there are hidden victory conditions, including a traitor type role, who wins if the everyone loses. 

Certainly interesting to play, but by no means one of the greats.  A few too many warts.

RATING: N/A

I had a couple of problems with my copy. First, the insert that is supposed to hold the tiles didn't work very well. And second, my cubes have incorrect colours, where they do not match the ones in the rulebook and the market/domestic board.

RATING: 8.54
An amazing and beautiful resource management game with emphasis to exploration of an ocean Archipelago. First we all begin with our ships on one hexagonal ocean tile (or desert tile as a variant). Then as we take turns we are laying more and more hexagonal tiles around, expanding our board, our lands and colonies. Then we employ natives to work on resource extraction, we build churches, markets and cities, we even fight the rebellion and/or other players. This game is so immersive and rich with theme. I love hexes and these are maybe the most beautiful I saw in any game. It has somewhat complex and clunky set of rules and we needed 3 or 4 sessions until we finally figured out all the rules and errors. But the game flows very nicely. Has lots of mechanics, like bidding, worker placement, tile laying and trade. And it's semi cooperative since all players must work together to keep stability and piece in the colonies. All except one possible Separatist, whose goal is to spread chaos and rebellion. We all hate that guy. Altogether one fine game I had to add to my collection.

[b]Update:[/b] Rating reduced to 8.7 from 9 due to general clankiness and little bits of maintenance.
[b]Update:[/b] Rating modified according to a custom rating system.
[b]Update:[/b] Raised from 8.44 to 8.60 due to great replayability.
[b]Update:[/b] Lowered from 8.60 to 8.54 when Learning Curve is decreased to 4 from 5.

[b]Favorite mechanism:[/b] Shared secret goals where only you know what will be scored at the end, but everybody can compete. Also, assembling the map of Archipelago as the game goes, by taking exploration action, choosing one hex tile and potentially adding it to the existing map.

RATING: 7
I love this game in theory and I love just about everything about it... Interesting economics, exploration, worker placement... But in my play group it has one fatal flaw.  The game forces players to cooperate when a crisis hits.  Failure to address the crisis carries a huge penalty and can bring about a shared loss for everyone if not addressed.  If you have a sore sport, or someone who likes to sabotage others, is bad at negotiating, would rather everyone suffer unless they get an advantage, or would rather see the game end in loss than you win, the game becomes incredibly not fun because there's is nothing to penalize them or stop them from doing so, in fact their is an end game objective that could encourage them to do this.

RATING: 2
What a mess of a game. It's a bunch of ideas thrown together but feels like nobody playtested it. When we played, nobody was motivated to bid on turn order; it didn't seem to make any difference. Why throw away your money if having the second highest bid doesn't guarantee you second choice of turn order? 

Nobody had motivation to buy evolution cards; with rare exception, they didn't seem to benefit single players, so we spent the whole game just rotating them. Boring. The few evolution cards that were helpful were so expensive as to be undesirable.  

Nobody had reason to use the foreign market.  Other than my own private win condition, I had no other goals, and so had no idea what I was supposed to be doing.  How can you plan for  3 to 4 other hidden scoring conditions?  My game-ending condition was to exhaust the discovery icons, which would have taken 4 to 5 hours to achieve.  One of the scoring conditions was boats, but I had no wood to build boats or other buildings in any of my tiles for half the game.

If you know you're doing worse than others, you are motivated to start a revolution in order to end the game and make everybody lose.

Most games click after you've played for a while. Archipelago never clicked.

RATING: 8
After one play, I like Archipelago a lot. I've a feeling this game may grow on me as I enjoy the hidden information aspects and the diverse use of Eurogame mechanisms.

A provisional score of 7, after one play.

This one will see a lot more play in 2013.

Score upgraded to an 8 (March 2013). Archipelago is growing on me as I experiment with the solitaire game and I'm very keen to try it again with 3 or more players. There are some very intriguing elements to the game - market management to mitigate against crises; the pressure to explore to generate Explorer Tokens / resources and the labour market consequences of exploration; ensuring access to and use of Evolution Cards; and, the interplay of port and market infrastructure when trying to maximising the benefit of commodity transactions.

There are diverse routes to victory, impacted necessarily by the emerging geography and position within that geography of a given player's citizens and infrastructure. 

The negotiation and hidden information elements add additional spice.

RATING: 3
Let me preface this by saying I think there is a good 90 minute game to be had somewhere within the game of Archipelago.  That game is *not* what it currently is though.

The game plays like the unnecessary parts were never edited.  The game has too many things going on, and has no elegance to it.  There is a mediocre market, with mediocre worker placement, mediocre resource management, and mediocre event phases.  Further, the scoring is downright bad.  Having a single open goal provides direction, but the point variation is too high with the hidden roles.  Points are very low, so end score is very swingy, as people are or are not completing objectives.

It was not a *bad* experience, but it offers absolutely nothing that some other game would not offer, and in a much shorter time span.  Not to mention, its pretty horrific theme.

RATING: 8
12/10/2012 - An interesting worker-placement and exploration game with a few interesting twists:
- As you explore you discover natives that would like to work. However, if there is not enough work then they become restless and can revolt - at which point everybody loses. 
- Each player gets a card that explains what will give bonus VPs for all players and an end-of-game condition that any player can trigger.
- you can score VPs by benefiting the hole colony. 
- the economic elements can work against you having too efficient an economic engine puts too many products in the markets which makes the natives restless. 

We played a 5-player short game so probably didn't explore the mechanic as much as we could - but it definitely needs a revisit.

RATING: 7
Very slow to get going as you have so many things you need to do initially and no workers with which to do it however after a couple of turns you are desperately trying to balance the unemployment (grey meeple) against the actions you have to take and the rebellion track (black meeple)rockets ever upward.  Rebellion is a really nice mechanic as it must be considered in EVERY action you undertake.
To 'control' rebellion you need to build a church but to build a church you need gold which requires taxing the population which increases rebellion.

Initially the events where you were required to spend goods to stand people up didn't really seem like a mechanic that had much of an effect and then suddenly one came up that we couldn't satisfy and boom rebellion overtook us and we all lost.

Really enjoyed this game and want to play it again soon, however it requires a massive table (we had to go to a local wargame store to use a wargaming table) and so I think its unlikely to hit the table all that often.
 
Really beautiful components and nice idea on the game insert, however it doesn't fit!

RATING: 9
Wow. What a story arc ! Impressive !

I enjoy this blend of resource management, guessing, semi-cooperation and overall, the tools given to players to shap the game at their will.

What i like into this game is the way the theme is driven by the mechanisms :

- exploration : it is a fog somewhere and you will have to adapt yourself if the resources doesn't meet your plans. Will you discover island or land ? This will drive your development (boats/fish and possible hard development vs more resources/buildings and easier development ?)

- population management : exploration -> new territories -> more people in the archipelago : recruitment, rebellion, taxation, birth ?

- crisis management : find a balance between overproduction/under production, needs of local people/homeland and rebellion.

Along with Antiquity, this is one of the first game that makes me feel the exploration time. Way more than Endeavor or Navegador that feel very dry to me.

This is certainely the epic exploration game i was looking for.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I so want to like this game, and I'm going to give it at least a couple more tries before passing judgement, but my first play was one of the biggest disappointments I've ever had in a game.  As a co-op game, it appears to be a very easy one - we were never anywhere near having the natives revolt in our first game, and what's more, there were never any rebellious workers either.  The most frustrating thing for me was the fact that while technically there's a lot that can be done on each turn, very little of it really seems to matter that much or be related to actually getting points.  There is a lot going on: exploration, market manipulation, acquisition of special powers through character/idea/technology cards, resource management, but I was surprised by how opaque the strategy was (I never say that about games) and how much of what the system allows for can end up being irrelevant.  The artwork is absolutely stunning though!  I'll update my comments when I've played it more.

RATING: 7
Nagyon sokáig állt a polcon a relatíve összetett szabálya, és a nagy játékidő miatt. Végre sikerült kipróbálni egy négyszemélyes játék keretei között. A rövid játékot próbáltuk a két kritikus cél nélkül, ám ebből rögtön kettőt is lenyomtunk. Nagyon ambivalens érzéseim voltak. Az egyedi mechanizmusok nagyon érdekesek, és a tematika, a hangulat nagyon magávalragadó.  Ennyire talán még sose éltem bele magam társasba felnőtt fejjel. Erre rásegít a semi-coop jelleg, ahol mindenkinek figyelembe kell venni a saját és köz hasznát egyaránt. Node ezzel el is értünk a problémámhoz. Egyrészt nagyon-nagyon meg kell válogatni, hogy kivel ül le az ember. A játékosoknak fel kell fognia, hogy a kollektív vereség nem jobb, mint a közös győzelem esetleg egy másik játékos abszolút győzelmével. Szóval ha valaki úgy ül neki, hogy inkább bedönti az egészet, ha ő nem nyerhet, akkor nem lesz élvezetes a játék. De megfelelő emberekkel nagyon izgi az, hogy úgy próbálom meg megmenteni/megmentettni a világunkat, hogy közben tyúklpésésekben azért haladjak a saját győzelmem felé is. Mi ebben a két játékban nagyon borotvaélen táncoltunk végig, így szinte egyáltalán nem jött ki a kompetitív jelleg, mert végig közdüttönk az életbemaradásért. Viszont utólag kiderült, hogy egy szabályt nem jól játsztottunk (az első körben nincs krízis), és ez lehet, hogy sokat könnyít majd a további játékokon. Bár szerintem még így is alakulhat úgy egy-egy játék a szerencételen krízisek miatt, hogy háttérbe kell szorítni a versengést.

Nagyon vágyom egy következő játékra a jó szabályokkal. Illetve mindenkinek ajánlom a játékot, akinek fontosabb, hogy a játék minden percét élvezze, és bele tudja magát élni a dologba, mintsem, hogy a végén egyedüli győztesként állhasson fel. Pszichológiai kísrlétnek se utolsó a játék. :)

Egyelőre 7 az értékelésem, de a helyes szabályokkal ez simán felugorhat 10-re egyből. :)

u.i.:
Az angol szabályfordítás sajna nem lett tökéletes (az eredeti francia), így faq-uk, player aidek bújása kötelező az első játék előtt, főleg enganged worker ügyben.

RATING: 7

RATING: 4
Archipelago combines a couple of elements that I don't like much so it's not particularly surprising that the game doesn't do it for me.

I'm actually beginning to think that semi-cooperative games cannot actually work, ever. (Of course, my definition of "works" might be rather different from yours here.) And while the traitor (or separatist as he's called here, true to the game's theme) does stir things up a bit he is making matters even worse. It is in every player's interest to keep the system on the brink of collapse because that means you'll have to pay less and can follow your own agenda as much as possible, and it's not difficult to see how a traitor would have little trouble exploiting this situation. Additionally, what's the point of being extortionist when the other guy can just say "screw you" and make everybody lose? And what about players who'd rather do that same thing rather than coming in third place or whatever?

Now add on top of this that you may have played a couple of hours when that happens, and that negotiation games aren't anywhere near my favourites to begin with and the few little touches that I do like here are drowned out by issues with the general game structure.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Random Thoughts:

This euro provides a good sense of a broad theme, but there are too many elements that feel removed from the theme resulting in a feeling that is not significantly more thematic than a "German" euro.  

The art and design gives 80s/90s adventure vibes, but is often not user friendly so I have to err on a lower score as a result.  For example, tile laying is rewarding and beautiful, but there is too much clutter on each tile so that the important information doesn't stand out as much.

Because of phase order, it is difficult to prepare for the archipelago balance events in advance leading to greater chaos (you may or may not like this).  Player behavior in board games typically adds to chaos and unpredictability already so I am not sure this was the best choice.  Also because of this phase order, these crises just happen, but there is no real reason or explanation, further hampered by the inability to anticipate.  As a result, there is not a sense that the play experience was as rich of a story as it could have been.   

There are many other mechanics that are not explained thematically or are just too dry.  In other words, actions often feel more mechanically-driven than theme-driven.  As someone who enjoys emergent narratives, I would prefer stronger thematic elements or a cleaner Euro.  If I have to deal with cognitive mess, there should be pungent thematic motivation.

Despite these flaws, there are still enough unique elements to keep me thinking about it and help it stand out among the crowd. I enjoy seeing the semi-cooperative element, the ability to select different objectives for shorter games, the diversity of secret objectives, and the openness of negotiation/bartering.  As a result,  the game does seem very gamer group dependent.  Even More plays will help me understand these aspects better.  Sweetspot is 3/4 players possibly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.9
Lots of great exploration, expansion, collection, and secret information. The secret objectives mean that no two games feel the same, because you have different goals every time. And it's tough to guess at your opponent's goals (although certainly doable) because all resources are valuable for various reasons in every game. Maybe he's collecting cows for points on his objective card... Or maybe he's trying to collect them to force his end-game condition. Or maybe he just wants to sell them on the market later for money. Or maybe he's trying to cover all of our bases before the next crisis...

The game length given for each set of cards is pretty accurate, so you can have short or long games. The game has some clunky mechanics but is still extremely fun to play.


The pieces are extraordinarily good looking and of good construction. The storage solution given in the box is horrible though. The plastic cracked almost immediately, which meant meeples slowly went missing until we figured it out. And the various components go everywhere in the box even when simply turning it sideways, much less transporting it.

RATING: 5
Only played once and the short version. And just as it was about to take off after 1h of rules explanations and 1.5h of gaming it ended. 

Here are my impressions and grade after that expericence.

- Hidden ending conditions are a terrible idea. Just terrible. You plan for doing something and suddenly the game ends. After playing it a bunch of times you might know all possible ending conditions but still. Horrible idea. 

- Feels old in many ways. It is much harder to explain then it should be. It is not streamlined or balances. It is just not very user friendly to new players. 

- Love the theme and exploring a modular board with huge hexes. Love that. 

- Love producing all you can of one resource with an action. Trying to get as much return as possible. 

- Like trading on two markets selling and buying goods. But you may with one action only trade one of a resource. And actions are very limited. You may buy building that lets you trade two resorces once per round for no actions. That is a must buy building to make money. It costs 2 to build and 1 to power so if you do not have the money you must spend an action to just get some money. 

- It is a semi-coop with all bad things that comes with it. Luckely it was very easy to beat the game so it did not matter in our game. Unlucky that my secret objective (you only get one not a choice at all) was to make us loose to the game. A thing that was totally impossible in our game. 

If I play it a few more times it will get better, but it will never get to be as good as my top ten favourite games to play instead in this situation...so that will never happen.

RATING: 6
I think this game is quite underrated. Its components and art design are exceptional. I feel excited about exploring this Caribbean world each game. The game is rich and has a lot of different mechanics built in (worker placement, exploration, trade, semi-coop, resource gathering, bluffing, war, race..). I like the fact that each player only knows some of the end game and victory conditions, forcing everyone to look at what the others are doing. Resources are scarce, so the race to gather them is fierce.
on the downside, I feel the game could have been more interactive and the semi-cooperative aspect feels under-used. Nowhere near Dead of Winter. Also. I like the fact the game is somewhat deterministic (no dice rolling) - only exploration is uncertain which is quite thematic. I am missing a good handful of dice though..
The small number of resources pushes the group to act fast, and focus their effort, preventing never-ending escalation of building one type of building or collecting one type of resources for instance. It is so pleasant to navigate on this map that the fact the game forces you to focus on your objectives can feel a bit frustrating at times. Also the cost of the wonders is so prohibitive that it makes little sense to build those unless seriously undermining your chances to catch up on the others..
Buildings are not that useful and all sorts of investments are quite poor. Especially having a second building of a certain type (a second market for instance). So, if you see someone building a second one, clearly, it is a victory or end game condition.. There is no tech tree for instance. But then the game stops at 5 of one type, making that race very repetitive and quite boring.. It would have been much better with 2, or different numbers on each card so it is unexpected.. keeping the game tense.. And the difference between short and long games is only the number of times you have to build the same building to finish the game, making long games completely boring.
Also, I thought that the card drafting mechanics were off-putting - you want to be able to interact with the gorgeous board as much as possible, not fiddle with cards..
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6

But it is a Euro, so no fighting, anyone.

RATING: 6
This one has my two favorite mechanisms, negotiation and exploration. Our game ended quickly so I didn't get a chance to really explore the game much. I think this could eventually make it up to a 7+ for me. The down side is that it is essentially a logistics game and you need to learn which actions to take in which order to maximize your efficiency. That isn't part of a game I particularly enjoy but it can be fun once mastered and you know how all elements of the game interact.

I like the unknown vp conditions, it prevents engine building and forces players to maximize points quickly instead of focus on creating a powerful engine an pumping out money or vp or whatever by the end. I also like the semi-coop, sacrifice this or risk everyone losing the game aspect. I think house ruling some elements like the explore tiles and possibly the card stack may enhance the players' ability to make strategic decisions but could stall the game.

Overall a fun euroized exploration/economy game that I would like to play again to get a better feel for. I think we just scratched the possibilities for negotiation on this one.

Well played it a second time and again the game ended after two turns, this time with no one winning. The good is the exploration and the card turning/buying mechanic. I still don't have a solid opinion on this one so I'd give it another shot.

RATING: 9
First try, intro game, 2p... not enough info to formulate an opinion.  Probably messed a few things up to cause a revolution at the beginning of Round 2?  :P

UPDATE:  Finally got around to playing this again.  I've watched so many playthroughs in an attempt to learn how to teach this game so I don't overwhelm my wife.  Basically we played the short scenario, "No Secrets Between Friends", completely open handed.  It served as a very gentle introduction to the main mechanical system of the game and the flow between phases.  Now that we understand how to play, we'll add the secret game-end conditions to add that stressor and make our actions more strategic.

UPDATE: First 4p game.  Amazing!  I used my teaching script (in the files section) and it was a resounding success.  2 non-gamers and 1 gamer learned the game by the second round and picked up on every nuance we encountered along the way.  Fantastic game.  Took 4 hours though :P (with the medium-length game)

RATING: 7
Interesting game, but wow, what a terrible way to end with the surprise private ending conditions--feels like the designer struggled to find a suitable game clock, and while the idea of partial information here is interesting, they are a terrible pairing with the Euro side of the game. Overall it gives the feeling of a design from the late oughts, whereas the design community have since created better ways to realize these design goals.

Other mechanics seemed interesting, but probably excessive hidden information with the assumption that it is required to support better player interaction. I'm not so sure, and many mechanics would have played out effectively the same with open information. While I was generally OK with it, one of the players in my group really disliked it.

Overall, there is some interesting stuff here, but I don't think the game deserves the rating it currently has (7.4 as of 9/2016).

RATING: 6
Like Earth Reborn, from the same designer: a really smart, wonky game almost no one wants to play. Teaching it is a bear, remembering it is just as bad, and one gets the impression throughout that there's a wholly artificial system at play, like trying to sleep on a sheet of cardboard painted expertly to look like a pillow.

As for the artwork and setting... At first, I thought it was a cheeky dig at games like Puerto Rico or Five Tribes, which mindlessly incorporate indentured laborers as part of the "abstraction" of game design. I was delighted at the prospect of a game in which your own workers can revolt against you! But statements on the site by the designer (In paraphrase: It's okay for me to be racist, I have a black girlfriend) lead me to believe maybe the designer has not wrapped his head fully around the issues this game evokes. And the arguments around this game on BGG tend to just descend into "racism exists/there's no such thing" idiocy. So, tetchy at best.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
This is a meaty game with great production value and an interesting theme. There is a lot to consider it has some great mechanics that combine spatial expansion, resource generation and worker placement with the economic element of buying/selling goods for profit and for the common good. The hidden end-game triggers and victory point awards are an interesting idea, but it can get a bit frustrating sometimes.

However, the semi-cooperative nature of this game just doesn't appeal to me. I do not buy the whole "primary goal is to prevent the rebellion" since the player with the most victory points is the grand winner. When there is a grand winner and all the rest are just winners, to me it sounds like one player won and the other have lost. Which brings me to the next point, which is related to the rebellion. The danger of the natives rebelling and everyone losing the game is ever-present and requires effort from all players to keep it at bay. So, once a player realises that there is not way for him/her to win the game, there is a great incentive to help bring about the rebellion and have everyone lose the game. Maybe it depends on your point of view, some people will be quite happy to play to their full ability, even if there is no whay for them to win. If you are in a competitive group however, I guess having someone causing the rebellion because there is no way they can win, will be something that will be occuring quite frequently.

RATING: 1
The problem with this game is that it has a good number of things that make it great if you and your group play the way it was meant to be played by the designer.  Take for instance the supplies end of game condition; it can happen so quickly that it can make the secret end of game conditions useless.
The secret objectives are also of wildly different value: knowing that any kind of good is worth points is more valuable that knowing, say, that the ports will give you points, because in the latter case owning more ports than the others is  an extremely difficult, quite random, proposition.
It seems to me that the game was playtested by like-minded players only. 

The game looks good, the game does many things quite simply and logically, but in the end can be very frustrating, because the secret winning conditions do not work (even though I love the idea), and because the turn order bidding breaks the game, and also because the semi-coop part does not work that much.
Too bad.

It gets a 1 as per the rating recommendations - the game is broken, although if I had to rate it against other games that would be a 4.

RATING: 8.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
(03/30/13) A very intriguing, open-ended, semi-cooperative, worker-placement game, blending a number of game mechanisms from many other classic games.

1) Exploration is reminiscent of Entdecker: New Horizons and/or Tikal. There is a stack of region hexes from which to draw from, with very strict placement rules. This results in a dynamic, unique, and colorful map board that unfolds beautifully.
2) Interesting and thought-provoking economics that is separated and decoupled into domestic and export markets, each with differing supply-demand values. (A comment was made that this market system would have made Supremacy a much better game.)
3) Reproduction reminiscent of the "Love Shack" mechanism in Stone Age. Two meeples in the same region can reproduce an offspring.
4) Evolution cards, which can be bought by the players, are reminiscent of building cards in Le Havre. Depending on the card, other players can "visit" and activate the card, paying a visit fee to the owning player.
5) Resource production similar to a number of other Euro-games. Meeples can "park" themselves onto resource icons on each region hex, with a harvest action. The twist is that all the player's meeples scattered throughout the archipelago can harvest one type of resource, if desired.
6) Four kinds of buildings can be bought per a resource schedule, similar to Settlers of Catan. Each can serve a different function.
7) The game is semi-cooperative, in that if the rebels exceed the civilian population, everybody loses, unless there is a player with a separatist secret objective card. This is similar to the "traitor" mechanic in Shadows Over Camelot or the "Cylon" in Battlestar Galactica: The Boardgame.
8) Crisis cards force players to cooperatively allocate resources to prevent ill-effects from taking effect. This is similar to "The Watch" mechanism in A Game of Thrones; everybody needs to contribute resources to help keep the Wilderings at bay.
9) Everything is negotiable, similar to Traders of Genoa. This can be rather uncomfortable for players who don't enjoy negotiation games, but the relative values of specific items is moderately discernible.

While there are probably several other "familiar" Euro-game mechanisms, the overall effect is an immersive game play, where all players must help maintain balance on the archipelago, lest chaos and rebellion are unleashed, resulting in the game "killing everyone". There is also a semi-civilization building aspect, because of the thematic exploration mechanic, exploitation of resources from the region hexes, and expansion in terms of buildings.

What makes the game unique is that each player has a secret objective card with two elements: top side indicates a secret game-ending condition, and a bottom side that indicates the victory point schedule for having the most of some element in the game, be it civilians, resources, money, etc. The game-end can be triggered by any one of the players' objective cards. However, all of the victory point schedules are considered and added up to determine the winner. The problem is that each player is only aware of their own victory point schedule. This makes for an interesting game of competing self-interests. Players must work together to ensure the game doesn't end in rebellion, while at the same time, doing whatever they can to promote their own self-interest, which is always at conflict with the overall well-being of the archipelago.

The rules are well-written, but dense in the typical Euro paragraph fashion. It took an hour to read through the rules together as a group. Ironically, the play itself is quite simple and straightforward. The components are top-notch, with cardboard punching out cleanly and quickly, and plenty of wooden components as well. The artwork, especially the map board, is simply gorgeous, but with wooden meeples and ships, unfortunately screams Settlers and Seafarers of Catan.

The initial play was rather clunky and unintuitive, but sped up after several turns. Clearly, many more plays are needed to fully understand the design and formulate a long-term strategy. The game was so open-ended in the different types of actions available, that players initially suffered mild analysis paralysis. However, most players were willing to see past the rough edges, and see this is a brilliant design, a diamond in the rough, so to speak. Patience and many plays look like they will amply reward players with successive plays. Initial rating is a "7", with room to go up.

Archipelago takes a smart worker-placement ruleset and through some concoction of modular elements creates something that goes beyond the tired "build the best victory point engine possible" gameplay of so many European-style designs.
