=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Archipelago is a rather controversial game.  The theme is that of European imperialist powers colonizing an archipelago of islands filled with natives.  It is a civilization genre game with worker placement (there is a board where you get to set out 3-5 discs on action spaces) where you expand the map by exploring, gathering resources, and then using them to build churches, ports, markets, towns, and boats or selling them for money.  There is a semi-coop element in that the discontent level of the natives tends to rise over time, exacerbated by crises every turn and certain otherwise lucrative actions.  If the natives revolt then everyone loses.  There are also wonders to be built and special tech/personality cards that provide useful abilities.  There is a lot of negotiation and bidding over turn order and who gets control of how the common resources are used to meet the constant crises.  The most contentious part of the game is that it has mostly secret scoring goals and end conditions with each player being dealt a card with one of each and then you have to figure out what cards the other players got.  This tends to be difficult unless you've played a lot, with the result that you didn't really know what to be working towards and the game ends unexpectedly.  I think this can be fixed with the simple (official) variant rule that all these cards are public knowledge instead of secret.  The only drawback is that doing so nullifies the interesting twist that one of the cards is a traitor card where you win if the natives revolt.  Perhaps one could have a further variant where there is always a traitor and allocation of that role is on a separate card that is secret.

RATING: 5.5
2013-02-21

Initial Rating: 7.0 (December 2012)

As my favorite game ever is Dungeon Twister, and I also really like the theme and gameplay of Earth Reborn, It was natural for me to try Archipelago. My first impression is that the game is great, it is a meaty game with many rules, but once you begin playing it, everything flows naturally and it is easier to play that you initially thought. Still the game has a satisfying depth so it is a keeper for me.

I don’t particularly like the theme, in fact, at least 2 friends refused to play Archipelago based on the theme alone, the theme doesn’t bother me, but it doesn’t excite me either. The gameplay is great, I love the negotiation required, the tension of the game, the crisis, the worker placement facet (there is so much to do and so little workers) and the way we can all lose if we don’t collaborate enough. Great.

The components are very nice (as usual), the art is also pretty cool.

On the bad side, the short game is not worth it, it finishes too quickly, better to begin with the medium game. Also I’m concerned with the luck factor inherent in the game, the exploration tiles has a lot of luck but I’m worried particularly about the end of game conditions and the variable objectives. I´m not sure about this, but It seems one player has an unfair advantage over the others when finishing the setup or when the game finishes. I would not like a meaty game like this one to be decided by luck, I will have to play again to see if this is a silly concern or if luck governs more than I like.  

For now I would like to play more Archipelago, this is another great game from a great designer. (Christophe Boelinger)

Current Rating: 7.0
[hr]
2013-10-28

I have played a couple 5 hour games (medium length), that’s way too long, however I also have played quicker games so the game length is very variable. I´m not sure how I feel about this. The time passes quickly though.

I’m downgrading its rating to a 6.0, because I feel the luck of the goals (and exploration) is more than I like. If a player has the same hidden goal and the same trend card, he has a very unfair advantage (7 VPs if first on that category) as one can almost always be first in a category if pursued single-mindedly.

Another issue, when a player is sure he is not winning the game, he can easily throw away the game via revolution, I dislike this but I have seen it happen twice. It is even worse in a 4 hour+ game.

I still like Archipelago a lot, however I usually don’t want to play it, so it is a 6.0 for me. A game that I would like to play  from time to time.

RATING: 8
Archipelago is my surprise hit of the Essen '12 season.

On first glance it appears to be a fairly straightforward worker placement exploration and market manipulation game.  The genius comes from two elements - the relentlessness of the rebellion and the hidden objectives.

Of the several "all players lose" semi-coop games I've had the pleasure of playing, Archipelago's loss mechanic is just about the harshest this side of Republic of Rome's Early Republic.  The domestic crises are capricious and will hit the players mercilessly unless beaten back by otherwise suboptimal domestic market sales.  This game requires unselfishness among the leaders, otherwise the rebels will win.  God help the colonists if one of the players has the Separatist goal.

Once the players are educated enough to keep the rebellion in check, the focus turns to how to become the "grand winner."  Archipelago borrows one of the more delightful mechanics from Troyes, the hidden objectives.  One can and should deduce other players' objectives once the game has passed a few turns.  It goes without saying that one's own hidden objective is paramount.

The special powers of the evolution cards are quite varied and look to provide a substantial amount of interplay variability as does the exploration.  After 5 plays I doubt I've seen a tenth of the different configurations this game has to offer.

The negotiation is fluid and though there is potential for backstabbing, there's also a lot of latitude for players to execute win-win deals with a little trust.

A solid 8, perhaps a 9 with a few more plays, and well deserving of a place in my collection.

RATING: 5
A buffet of modern euro mechanisms that surprisingly works, though I find it a bit overwrought for my tastes. The game features an interesting push and pull core; the mechanisms pull you toward long-term engine-building while the hidden-game-ending triggers/VPs push you toward a short-term sprint to the top.

Several members of my group really like Archipelago. I enjoy the concept more than its execution.

RATING: 6
A strange game. Many new mechanics and the semi-coop aspect is a real threat unlike some other games. But it's hard to have a sense of progression in this game when there's so much unknown. Most of time you feel like treading water until the game ends suddenly.

The exploration aspect is physically the most annoying part of the game, because it's hard to visualize if a tile fits the desired location without taking out the tile (since you can't reveal the next one).

The components are somewhat poor quality, very thin boards and tokens, and the main board is too small.

Will definitely try again to get a better sense of this one.

RATING: N/A
High learning curved worker placement exploration game. It took is like 3 games to figure out how badly we'd screwed up the rules.

I've seen professional game reviewers on youtube blatantly play this game wrong and nobody even notices.

Still, it's a beautiful game (especially on TTS) and it really makes you think. The supply/desmand economy tracks are a great and original way of introducing dynamic economies to the board. But yes, consider this one a time sink. One of the games prevailing virtues is that there are short, middle, and long variations to choose from, allowing for different time commitments/depth -- the expansion adds a lot more of an aggressive element if that's your thing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Archipelago is a group-dependent game with negotiations being one of the elements. To take off, it needs experienced players who use negotiations to blackmail, bribe, charm or betray other players. If players play mostly for themselves, the game has serious potential to tank. It also includes hidden victory and game end conditions. These could be deduced for probably half of the players by their actions but some uncertainty remains. It's no game for control freaks. It's possibly best for Eurogamers who like themselves some conflict or Ameritrashers who occasionally dive into Euro waters which this time are situated in the Caribbean thematically. 
Actually, theme is one of its strengths. Other strengths include variety, strategic depth and suspense regarding the conditions in play. Luck of the draw can be a drawback as well as a rulebook that leaves players with a lot of ambiguities. It's more of a framework within which players interact rather than a tight frame that is played by the players (e.g. Terra Mystica). 
I enjoyed my first play and would join again if it is the right group. Not sure though if this will rise or fall with further plays.

RATING: 6
I can see why some people would like this game. It is definitely meaty, there are a lot of things going on and a lot of choices to make on your turn.

However, I really dislike the cooperative aspect of the game. I don't have a blanket dislike for games where there is an "all lose" possibility. Terra is an example of a game where I feel it's handled pretty well. The problem in Archipelago is that there's not very much incentive to try and ward off the losing condition. In our game, players were being greedy, and though I could have prevented us from losing, I decided to just give up and let us lose the game. I realize that this is not in the spirit of the game, but at least in our group, I can't see it ever working out any differently.

RATING: 6
A semi-cooperative action selection game, with hidden scoring. Players take turns moving around and exploring an archipelago, building up the island via towns, temples, and ports, and buying/selling on the domestic and export markets. The game ends once one of a player's secret objectives is completed. I didn't care for the semi-cooperative aspect of the game and the hidden scoring, but otherwise the core mechanics of the game are solid.

RATING: 7

Every game will be long.

RATING: 6
I love the artwork and ideas in this game, and I think it is an experience most interested gamers should give a go. It is a unique mix.

However for the time you spend playing the game you often feel a little short changed. This is primarily due to the hidden game ending and victory point conditions, which can make the ending come as a slight shock, and the dilution (by time) of interesting individual player decisions by the auctions and negotiations (as well as the updating of the various boards).

With some efficiency in bookkeeping this game really shines, but ultimately falls slightly short for me.

RATING: 7.5

What I like the most about this game is also its detriment: the hidden end-game conditions.  In my experience, the game is just SO LONG, and this is really the main factor keeping this from getting more play.

I can definitely see this being a great game for the right crowd, but this game is definitely not for me. It's not even just the fact that I'm not a fan of such open negotiation in games, or that you need a very specific play style in the group so someone doesn't just tank the semi-coop aspect if they are losing. There's also the metagaming of who has what scoring conditions (with no player aids to help with the attempted deduction), but most importantly the rules in this game feel very unintuitive. For example, the pricing of the special powers cards. The turning mechanism to change price and mill the market feels very tacked on and since you also turn to activate the ability it feels like thematically the two should have been tied better together. There's also the bidding for turn order (which admittedly is in a lot of Euros these days, but the addition here adds another layer of mechanism discord with the theme), exactly why the markets and workers ready to hire are tied with the rebellion track, and the messy looking worker placement board (also not regarding its level of intuitiveness, but I wish there was more blocking in the worker placement aspect). Overall this lack of intuitiveness caused the game to really have no narrative for me. Finally, there are many little things in the rules that if you forget one of them can spell disaster to the balance of the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Great game idea but not that good executed. Some mechanics are redundant, others are being disliked (you pay for a card, the opponent benefits of it ??)
Still thinking about house rules that make the game even better.

RATING: 8.6
A wonderful game.  At its heart it is simply a Euro Worker Placement game, a type of game that usually leaves me indifferent.  However, the game takes this basic Worker Placement mechanic and elevates in in many ways.  It has a strong theme, excellent production values (except for the coins), strong interaction, and good mechanics.  It's easy to teach and play, with little downtime.  You can focus on the game, and have many options and freedom to play the game as you like.  I like the hidden shared victory conditions, and the possibility of having a traitor.  The semi-coop works well, the game feels competitive but also cooperative  A game with depth, immersion, interaction, beautiful components, and intrigue.  Definitely a keeper in my collection, I look forward to returning to this archipelago often.

RATING: 10
Despite being bad at winning this game, it's still a lot of fun to play.  Archipelago is about the journey.  The design is just gorgeous, physically and gameplay-wise.  Everything is bright and colorful.  The domestic and export markets, as well as the worker and dissident pools, function in an interesting and LOGICAL fashion. Of course letting the unemployed pile up creates people who aren't happy with you!  You bought all the fish on the market?  Great, thanks, now it's expensive.

The rulebook is terrifying, but really, it's set up to be logical and to follow a nice flow.  Playing it is a breeze.

You and your friends will play together in mostly a jovial and peaceful fashion (usually...), all the while scrutinizing everyone's actions and occasionally pressing your luck with others' kindness.  Fun and interesting.

A good experience, overall, but it probably won't become a favorite.  I put this somewhere in the "7" range right now.

RATING: 5
(comments based on short game)

Beautifully produced game with good ideas, but some gaping holes in the mechanics which lead to dead ends. The foreign market seems extraneous, lucky/unlucky tile draws can seriously swing the game balance, a single player can end the game on a whim and cause everyone to lose, the market can be bled dry of resources with no way to re-stock. I really dislike how three of the five victory/end game condition cards are unknown to each player, so that you score (or don't) mostly by accident and how turn order is bid on, but you always lose the money you bid, even if you lost the auction!

Simply, the game has too many 'leaks' and ways to grind to a halt, or be 'crashed' by players and it never feels like you know what you're aiming for. Maybe in the longer game (if you knew all the cards) you could guess other players' victory conditions.

(after more plays)
I am coming around a bit to this one, but I still don't like the ability of a single player to hog a resource and deny it to everyone - Soak up all the stone and nobody can even build! Much better if you know all the potential scoring and end-game conditions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I really really wanted to like this one, and to some extent it intrigues me - especially as to why it just doesn't quite work for me.

A rather simple but fiddly worker-placement system (with barely any blocking) drives the board play which is a shared set of hexes making up the Archipelago. Hidden goal cards for every player means that guesstimating your overall standing is very difficult. A shared loss condition is an overarching threat throughout the game. I love the idea, that players who feel left out of contention can drag down those that are, either forcing the draw or levelling the playing field. Then, when a player feels ahead, they must strike to end the game before the cycle repeats, either through their known end condition or what they suspect might be someone elses.

Now, why doesn't it work for me and my group? Well, first, mostly due to my group. This game is fragile, with a capital crystal F. At any point, if a player doesn't care that much about the game, the game is over and done. Someone who does something just "because it would be fun", and the game goes to pieces. Fine, don't play with those people (easier said than done for me). Second, and this is more my personal qualms with the game - the blind bidding is a turn-off, requiring too many plays to really understand the value of (which again, results in the game breaking as others fail to see the point and start mucking about) - also, the pace of the game, each action drives the game but an inch and the board as a whole feels meaningless, the cost to move away from your original position is too great, the time spent setting up a greater play is rarely worth it - also, there are no "greater" plays in terms of effect - it's always "get one fruit", "get two stone", "build a church", "build a port", never a surprise and never anything well, exciting? Interaction on the board is a costly affair, seldom worth it. And the granularity in the scoring, well it isn't there. One stone, one church, one card, and you can get zero points or all the points. And you are supposed to infer your standing based on this? I suppose it could be done, but ye... I guess ultimately the game might be too tight, and too loose all at once, I can't even rant very coherently about it...

Meh, it just left me with a meh,. The core concept is intriguing, the mechanisms sound (well, on the surface at least) and the end result should have been something great, but it isn't. Not for me and the people I play with. Maybe someday, with someone else? I hope so.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Archipelago is a mish-mash of your usual Euro mechanisms grafted onto a couple twists that were theoretically interesting but failed in practice for me. First was the semi-cooperative avoidance of a "rebellion," which ultimately just taxes the player(s) in the lead: if you're already losing, you don't care, but if you think you'll win, you are forced to help put down the revolt. Second are the secret endgame and scoring conditions each player is dealt at the start of the game. I'm not sure what kind of game play this is supposed to inspire, but it really took a lot away from the game for me. Instead of feeling like I should explore the myriad options available to me, I felt locked into whatever my card said. Moreover, the scoring condition on the public scoring card happened to match resources on the tile I'd "explored" during the game set-up, so I had a huge advantage before the game even began. Of course, this might peter out over the course of a long game, but really how long can anyone endure converting cubes into money, tiles, cards, and other cubes?

Thus, on game play alone, I really have no interest in ever playing this again, which would get a 3 from me. But then there's the theme, which takes this into the realm of visceral loathing. Call me "sensitive" or hypocritical--somehow I don't have a problem with the Slave counters in Struggle of Empires and have managed to excuse the brown "colonists" brought on ships to work the fields in Puerto Rico--but this was just too much. The game puts players in the position of implicitly celebrating the most insidious and patriarchal aspects of colonial imperialism. Players "lose" if the natives gain independence. Religion, "education," and hard labor are all tools to pacify a primitive population apparently doing nothing worthwhile prior to occupation. This deplorable theme made this game difficult for me to play, and I hope I never come anywhere near it again.

RATING: 6
I really do quite like this game - very close to love. It has some very interesting ideas, and it feels extremely different from any other game I've played. There's so much open space - freedom. In some ways it feels like the polar opposite of Agricola, which has a cold, unwavering 14 turns and a goal of having "a fair deal of everything." This game could end at any time, and the goals are extremely specific and different each game. It is refreshing in some ways, but I do feel like I could never take this game "seriously" in the same way I would take most euros, as the winner is often as surprised as everyone else that he won. Definitely worth playing and getting to know this system though, there's a lot of cool stuff going on!

UPDATE: The excitement wore off really fast on this one - gameplay is too messy, too many vague rules, and takes forever to teach to new players for an experience that just isn't really worth it. From a 9 to a 6 - may trade this one away.

UPDATE: Traded for Suburbia

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.3
[b]Archipelago[/b] by [person=1206][/person] (Dungeon Twister, Earth Reborn...)

[image=1640388][/image]

2-5 players (or 1 with the solo expansion)

Expansions:
- Solo 
- War&Peace 
- a [url=https://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/21766819#21766819]new expansion[/url] with 18 new tiles is in the works (the French Caribbean island of Guadeloupe is mentioned in Essen 2015 interview)

Archipelago is a unique game, a keeper for me, which is getting played. Due to its nature (modular board, numerous evolution cards and game mechanics, varying objectives), every game will be different. I can also imagine easily house-ruling this one to make it even better. Yes, I believe there is great potential here to improve an already great game, and the solid rulebook needs to be improved upon (by Paul Grogan?).

The game also seems to be very well suited to future expansions. So far, there are 2 expansions: Solo and War&Peace. I have not come across any newer game, which is similar that could replace it.

True, it has a number of things to be constantly aware of to do well in multi-player (such as the 10 hidden objectives), but as far as rules go it's no Mage Knight, it is more a Caylus. There are some rules that might need researching in the forums, so for future reference take notes whenever something odd has been clarified to you. 

Thus far, I have played only solo and 2-player games. 

-Solo has been an entertaining puzzle, and a good way to learn the game (Note: for solo games you must purchase the solo expansion)

-2-player has been competitive fun, and avoiding a rebellion has been both challenging and a source of negotiation. My gf and I love this game.

-3-5 players. I expect more players shall have more varied negotiations and a new dimension of diplomacy shall be added. A sense of suspicion as well, since one of the player's game objectives may be to incite a rebellion. A timer might be necessary for players with analysis paralysis.

As with Tigris & Euphrates, though via different mechanism (the hidden objective cards), I like how you don't know who is leading the game till the very end, but can only make an educated guess (there are 10 possible objectives), that is, unless you are able to hire the Spy to seek out more information on your opponents´ objectives.

Update:
Haven´t played this enough times yet, and mostly 2-player, but it feels like it ends too quickly, just when you start to enjoy your "empire building". It has to do with the hidden objectives, which can be at times too easy to attain. Perhaps it's just player inexperience, but if game play doesn't develop into a slightly longer back-and-forth struggle, the game will need more tweaking to make it truly shine. As such it feels too much like a race. But perhaps that would then be another game, or something for an expansion to address.

Possible "fixes": 
1) for objectives choose a higher player count (ex. for 2-player games, use the objectives for 3-players)

2) trend card (the public objective) could be replaced with  harder objectives, which you can not simply race for (such as X fish), but must struggle over (such as...?).

Excellent review by Chally:
https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/947559/what-youre-missing-archipelago

[b]Essential rule clarifications:[/b]
https://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/948833/what-you-might-be-doing-wrong-summary-most-common

Rules:
http://download.ludically.com/ArchipelagoSite/Archipelago.Rulebook.EN.web.pdf

Mobile phone version rules:
http://download.ludically.com/ArchipelagoSite/Archipelago.Rulebook.EN.mobile.pdf


Card number and size:
42 Standard 63.5 x 88mm
48 Square 70 x 70mm

RATING: 5
Semikooperative Spiele sind nicht so mein Ding. Das musste ich bei Archipelago erneut feststellen. Zu oft kommt die Frage auf "Warum sollte ich das jetzt machen? Warum sollte ich mich aufopfern für die Runde, und damit Rohstoffe einbüßen?". RATING: 7
There's a lot to get your head around in this game at the start, but once you've had a round or two then you get the hang of it.

I think what's hardest to understand is that EVERYTHING can be negotiated. Turn order is just the start. The game rounds can play very fast too, the limited number of actions you can do means that you are not really stuck with left over actions that you need to find something useful to do with.

However, there is a lot of luck in the game, if you do not get a decent spread of resources then you will have to spend actions exploring in the hope you get the tiles, or actions on the market/port to get those resources.

Upon reflection, the hidden end game conditions are quite irritating and give you no direction to move in, you just have to study the manual all game and hope you can work out what the other players are doing (usually too late to affect anything).

RATING: 9.25
There is absolutely no individual component in Archipelago that you haven't seen before, right down to the generic meeples.  Yet Archipelago is one of my favorites.

Not since Endeavor has a Euro-game so expertly captured the feel of colonization (there's probably a wargame or two that does a better job).  Each game tells a story as dictated by the trend card and endgame cards.  Our first told of the masses coming to the islands; our last game was one of proselytization and converting the natives.  Every element of gameplay feeds into this epic tale.  There are tight choices that stand before you each turn with your limited resources and actions.  You need to stay focused on the known victory conditions, yet you can never know them all... and so you must diversify.  You'll need to wheel and deal with your collaborators in order to come out on top.  And you'll need to watch your colleagues like a predator and see what they cherish in order to do likewise.

Because at the heart of this admittedly messy Frankenstein's Monster combination of mechanics is a game of politics.  You need to take steps to secure victory while simultaneously keeping the natives at peace -- not an easy task.  They may as well called this game The Republic of the West Indies.

Finally, the artwork is insane.  You're getting your moneys worth here in bits alone, much less the narratives and the intense politicking that Archipelago will invoke.

People have been asking for decades when there will be a Civ killer.  I'm calling the body at 6:26 CST, November 5, 2012.  It's not going to be everyone's cuppa, but Archipelago is my new Civ.

RATING: 7.4
Great game, many decisions, many paths to victory, many random elements to grant replayability and it's very difficult to lose all hopes of winning until the very end, because you don't know other people's objectives...

It's not a thematic game, but the theme is there, it leads you to believe you are colonizing an acrhipelago and all the mechanics are very well integrated with the theme.

All the elements work very well, the actions, the materials, the economy, the progress cards, the buildings, the map tiles, they all settle in very well in global game.

I was quite concerned about the bad luck with the tiles distribution, but I was surprised at how well it managed not having a decent variety of resources (I almost won my first game [tied, in fact] after having only pineapples in my 3 tiles during almost all the game). Much better than some great games like Eclipse and Twilight Imperium.

The art is quite good, I love everything but the circular board...


One big problem: it's semi-cooperative. I don't like this kind of games, I don't enjoy the ambiance they create and several other consecuences, but this is not as bad as others, because people won't lose their hope until the very end, all are motivated to contribute towards the global well-being.

A secondary problem is its lenght.

I would rate it at least 1 point higher if it were not a semi-cooperative. Still, the best semi-cooperative I've ever tried :-)

Still, it's a game I bough because of the solo experience (still to be tested) and I'm willing to play again with real people :)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This game has serious identity issues. At the first glance, the box size and price tag deliver a false promise of plastic pirates and dice rolling. On a second look, the huge amount of wooden pieces and market matrices make you expect some heavy-duty resource management.

When you get to the rulebook, it soon becomes clear how completely the attempts at adding a thematic game on top the resource management derail the entire package: 
- Rulebook is full of holes
- Blind bidding for turn order
- Free negotiation
- 'Everybody loses' end condition
- Horribly out of place, chaotic and complex special power cards

And finally, during the game, other failings become obvious:
- Poor and confusing graphic design. The most glaring issue, but certainly not the only one, is choosing not to use the native head icons on the island tiles.
- If a player wants to throw a game and ruin it for everyone, rushing for either his personal secret end condition or the 'everybody loses' option is too easy.

Also, the fact that the action selection scheme does not restrict other players from choosing the same action just does not feel right. There probably is a reason for such a non-blocking system (for example, meeple movements on island tiles actually do create some blockage/timing considerations) but for my group it just made the gameplay feel unsatisfactory. And the huge swings in the end scoring even more so.

Expansions:
War & Peace

Verdict:

What I Like: The chance of a traitor being in the midst. The strangeness of playing two games at once, a cooperative survival game and a competitive civ game. Observing how this creates a dual mind with twisted motives in both myself and others, especially considering the free trade/bribery rule. The tile art is stunningly gorgeous when connected into a cohesive map. The hidden objectives add a fun deduction/bluffing aspect to the game.

What I Don't Like: So many fiddly bits to move and track! The short game is way too short. The medium game is too long. Not ever going to try the long game. This game will not work well with some people due to its above mentioned two-game nature. Can lead to hurt feelings through broken trust.

RATING: 7
I'm not sure what to make of Archipelago. On the one hand, it's got some very clever things going on, including an interactive market system, competition for resources, and (when it works) a nifty 'shared burden/shared loss' effect (the crises that various players can choose to help fend off or not).

On the other hand, the auction system is pretty awful, the character cards are very swingy, and there is an overarching fragility to the design that makes me think the game will only work if players play the 'right way'.

Need to play more to see which side of the fence I end up on, but for now this is an impressive effort. Time will tell if the game ends up as glorious or a glorious failure.

RATING: N/A
1200 已拆未玩
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
We played two short games of it tonight, four players each time. The first "short" game ran about 3 hours, the second took about 90 minutes. There's some fussiness we had to think/talk through on the first game, but it's by no means "hard." Mostly it's a matter of looking up or thinking through individual card effects. Most of our questions were in fact in the rulebook; the FAQ and the "rules you're getting wrong" threads and the player aids were not necessary.

The goal of the game is to be the smartest player about guessing at what will score and what will end the game. Everyone gets a secret objective card that has both a game-end condition and a scoring condition. There's also a "Trend" card that is public, that anyone can score on. 

Absent that information, the game's economies themselves are mostly divorced from any intrinsic value. Money, as an economy, is universally valuable: It's what buys you the power to set the turn order, it's needed to build some of the scoring assets, and it's how you buy the "Evolution" cards (special bennies that you can trigger without using up your oh-so-valuable action chits). But food economies are mostly there to feed the locals and make money, and materials like stone, wood and iron are there to build up larger assets (towns, markets, ports, churches).

So it's kind of weird, right? Building this economy without any sense, initially, of where the game may be going. Making money isn't hard but it takes people. Making people isn't hard but it takes actions. Getting resources takes people and actions. Building stuff is money and people and a very small investment in goods. Feeding the locals is mostly a matter of banking food, but someone has to be willing to do that. And so on and so forth.

The killer app of the game is the thing it repurposes from Republic of Rome, and that's the "everyone loses" condition: when the angry locals exceed the peaceful colonists, game over, go home, you're dead. The extra twist is that there's a chance that someone at the table will win only if everyone else loses. The traitor thing is very cute, even though we never saw a traitor in either game.

We haven't played nearly enough of this game yet, but in the short game I think it's hard-to-impossible to really suss out each others' secret cards. One big reason is that nearly every endgame condition is someone else's scoring condition. So as you're evaluating someone's play, you have to decide "Hey, is that dude building churches because he gets VPs for having the most churches? Or is he trying to run down the clock because whatever his real goal is, he's beating us all?"

The additional twist to all this is that everyone gets to score on everyone's secret goals. This can be frustrating when someone wanders along and just totally blows you out of the water on your secret, utterly unaware that they've done it. In the second game, I had the "most money" objective, for example: Well duh, dude, everyone is desperate for money! So someone raked in a giant pile and I was aced out of my own secret card.

We're going to try it again next week with the medium-length game, again with four players, and see if the signals percolate to the surface a little more clearly with more time to play. In both cases the game came to a sudden end, and in both cases the winning scores seemed ...arbitrary. We didn't feel like we'd particularly earned a victory or a loss based on good or bad play.

What is certainly true is that the gameplay experience itself is fun. This design space is something I've been working on myself, where the goal of the game's design is to manipulate the social interactions at the table and not just balance spreadsheets or grub together bigger die pools. Exploring is fun, working your economy is fun, second-guessing each other is fun, haggling and deal-brokering is fun. It's all fun! But we're not sure yet if it's satisfying.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Interesting and engaging game. One common VP race and one secret VP race for each player, where each player scores in each VP race, means no one is quite sure who's in the lead. Just start building and acquiring stuff that other people seem to be building and acquiring. Which sounds easy but isn't because each player's board position makes some things easy re resource acquisition and others hard. There's lots of worker placement avenues so there's not the usual high WP tension ... it's more of a means to your own sandbox end which seems to allow a number of different approaches as to how you acquire resources and money. The natural thing to do is have lots of babies, but you quickly find out that that leads to everyone losing ... as each card-fuelled crisis requires spending resources per people on the board. So there is a collective semi-coop aspect re expanding sensibly, as much as the land can bear I guess, which I don't mind too much because no one knows who's leading so there isn't an aspect of I'm losing therefore I'll kill the game off. It's a level removed, and it helps govern a bigger picture. It's done nicely. The game feels like it might play differently each time, and it's not round-bound, which are two big pluses in my Euro book, so I'm happy exploring this further.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Like others have said this is a gamers game. It is difficult to explain the game to others and takes time to do so. The most complex game I've played since Earth Reborn.

1st game will have everyone fumbling around trying to figure out how to play and what to do. It doesn't help that there is some confusion in the interpretation of the rules. 

By the time the 1st game gets near the end, the game will begin to come together and make sense. I found it an interesting and fun game. I actually didn't want it to end when it did and we played for 3hours. We played a 5 player game and two of us liked it, one reserved judgement till they could read through the rules themselves and the other two would rather commit ritual suicide then play again. The two that didn't like the game found it too long and overly complex for what it is.

It just doesn't seem possible to grasp the game in one session. This is a game that needs to be played a few times for it to click. Unfortunately most people won't make that kind of investment so this is a game that will be hard to get to the table. I hope people will give this game a chance.

I'm trying to figure out how to explain this game to others without boring them to tears. It might be best to show an example round or two so they can visually see it working. This game is in desperate need of a summary and player aids.

I hope I can get my wife to play the game, but I think she would be overwhelmed by the numerous choices and the initial ambiguity in formulating what to do next. 

I don't think this game is getting played again at my FLGS unfortunately as it didn't go over well with the Alpha players. Not sure if I will keep the game or not. It's a good game but it's polarizing and there is no point keeping a game no one will play.


edit: returned this one back to the store. While I liked it the pain threshold for learning this game is high. It's also difficult to teach without having everyone's eyes glaze over. It requires some dedication and investment by the players, and honestly I'm not sure the game needed to be this way to accomplish what it set out to do. 

Also there is no point in keeping the game if no one is going to play it with you. If the one person who still has their copy at my flgs game nights wants to play two or three player then I'll get my fix from him. I may pick the game up again when the expansion is available. The game should have included the solo rules rather then making people have to purchase an expansion to get them.

RATING: 9.5
Probably my favorite game aside from Pandemic Legacy.  I love building the world with tile laying.  I love the semi-cooperative nature of the game that forces you to help and receive help from your opponents.  I adore the hidden victory conditions component.  I don't struggle with the black meeple as some complain about because it is not just a representation of native unrest but also of homeland countrymen's unrest.  There is just so much going on in this game that prioritizing and trying to minimize negative consequences of intentionally ignored actions is foundational to being successful.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I’ve been wanting to try this for quite a while now, and I’m happy to report that I love it. I’ve described it as a sandbox game, where players have a huge number of choices and the best path forward isn’t always clear. This is something I tend to enjoy quite a bit in games. Archipelago has a lot of unknown elements - players only know two of the conditions that will score points at the end of the game, and must attempt to deduce the others by watching his opponents. The modular hex board starts very small, but quickly expands via exploration. Because the resources and land/sea configuration of new tiles are either partially or completely unknown, players must balance the need to explore with the uncertainty of success. 

The game has not one, but two resources markets that players can use to buy and sell goods at fluctuating prices. Players can build building and acquire cards that grant special abilities. There is just so much about this game that is clever. It’s commonly called semi-cooperative, but I think that does the game a disservice. There are some truly mean things players can do in the game. I think Archipelago is better described as a competitive game in which everyone can lose. 

So far, I’ve only played the short game, but there is still quite a bit to explore there. I think this is a keeper for now.

RATING: 7
The game has a very good balance between the individual game and the coop game. It's a lot of fun, you can really feel the theme and it has a high replay value. I highly recommend this game.

It is not a game for highly competitive matches, as the system is not tight that way. 
The rules can require two passes to fully understand, but even if you get a few wrong during your first match it'll still be fun, and once you have one person who understands the rules well enough it's easy (but a little long) to explain them to new players.

The rebellion marker has not been as much an issue as I would've liked so far but I only played the shorter games for now. 

Although it says 2 to 5 players, I'd say the sweet-spot is 3 to 4 players (4 being a little better). The 2 player rules is basically each player controlling 2 players, so it ends up being a 4 player match with 2 players.

RATING: 5
Update: Raised from 3 to 5. Played another time, short game, three players.

We intentionally removed the "traitor" goal from the game. We also had a much better understanding of how all players can lose.

I think this game suffers in BGG ratings because it will undoubtedly give a very bad first impression. The short game will end too abruptly for most players, or the rebels will win without players noticing. Although the game itself is straightforward, there is *a lot* of chrome added to it.

Also, I think this game shines only if all players know all the possible winning conditions. They are all printed in the rules so that players can refer to them; it would have been much better if this list was available to all players as a player aid.

Still, I'm not sure if I would want to play the medium-length game. I didn't find the game itself that compelling -- it's often frustrating because the game restricts players from doing what they want to do.

=====

The reviews for this game don't reflect how simple this game is. Place hexagon tiles to create a map, move dudes on them, produce resources, sell them, build buildings on them, purchase new dudes or let them reproduce. All of these give VPs. The trick here is that only some of them count towards winning. And players don't know what all of them are.

As for the semi-cooperative part: I like this mechanic in general in which players have to divide their resources between solving a puzzle cooperatively or getting VPs. Players who are not in the lead have to coerce those who are to put more effort into the puzzle (and less into themselves), which should help balance the game. 

Unfortunately, it feels like a typical Euro, a genre of which I'm not really a fan: Multiple, yet limited and uninteresting paths to victory and way, way, way too fiddly for what it is. In addition, I didn't find this cooperative puzzle -- to prevent a rebellion -- that compelling. 

In the only game I played, I believe we didn't fully understand what was required from all players to prevent the game from winning.

Gorgeous art on the hexagonal map tiles.

RATING: 7.5
It is such a huge hurdle finding a group of gamers who appreciate the delicate semi-cooperative balance in Archipelago. The island resources and the angry natives are in a constant state of flux, and each player is secretly hoarding something, meanwhile, worrying about the need to hoard everything else just in case it's someone elses secret goal.

We played with the idea that is better to make deals and call in favors than play as violent explorers. There's plenty of island to go around, and try to remember that 4th place is still better than being murdered by natives. With like-minded gamers you're bound to find a unique, challenging, and rewarding experience.

RATING: N/A
I won't rate this as I've only played it once, as a five-player game. I imagine I'd end up giving it a 5.

I won, more by luck than judgement, but didn't really enjoy it.

The game does have some nice interrelating elements and knock-on effects, with actions causing ratios to change or otherwise adding/reducing pressure elsewhere. So if you like the idea of a 'Rube Goldberg machine' then you might love this. There is plenty to think about and plenty of options, if you can be bothered to navigate the fog of moving cubes, hidden goals, random events etc.

My biggest problem is its slow crawl of buying/selling cubes, watching closely who's acquiring what cubes, checking various scales to try to buy things before they become more expensive etc. It means that playing the game 'seriously' would be like monitoring 20 CCTV screens at once ... just to gain a small advantage amid all the randomness. Personally, I like to see more exciting/reliable results if I put that much effort into a game.

There are some awkward mechanisms:
- End-of-turn objectives that are hard to plan for because they can randomly change.
- Sudden endgame triggers/conditions that undermine your ability to 'pace' your strategy.
- The anticlimactic ability to intentionally trigger an 'everyone loses' ending, without huge difficulty, if you're behind and don't want others to win.
- Too much hidden information to get a good/fun sense of whether your actions are affecting other players much.
- Negotiation is massively open-ended and can be done as much as you like, but it slows the game to a snail's pace.
- Etc.

Overall, I can't be bothered with this much laborious squinting.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Archipelago at first seems like a very solid Euro game with excellent mechanics that fit very well to the theme: you explore the islands, gather resources, trade them in for money, increase your population, build buildings, receive  taxes. In all your actions you have to watch out that you do not increase the global revolution level too much, or everybody loses. At the beginning of each round there is some crisis in which the players have to pay some resources to avert it.

Every player receives secretly a game ending condition (e.g. number of certain buildings build) and victory point conditions. The game ends, when one condition is met and then each player gets victory points according to ALL players' VP condition. 

In general I like the game since the mechanics are really excellent and make thematic sense. Furthermore the board (tiles) and components are beautiful. But I have some issues with a few aspects. Blind bidding for turn-order with winner-takes-it-all and everyone loses his bet is just not fun; being first actually matters quite a bit, especially for the crisises. I actually do not have a big problem with the semi-cooperative aspect; we play with VP awarded for goods donated during crisis, so most of the time it is rational to donate. My biggest complain is the  hidden ending and VP conditions. Two of my games were over too abruptly, when you just got going (even for a "medium" length condition). There are so few VP awarded and you can score by luck VP by your opponent's condition, such that there is a rather big luck component in determining the winner. Probably it would be better to have a sheet with all ending conditions, such that you actively avoid any of these to keep the game going. But this would be so unthematic and gamey that I do not want to take this effort.

[i]Update:[/i] Rating decreased from 7 to 6

While playing the game is a lot of fun, in 4 plays I have never seen a satisfying ending. Either the end came unexpected when you got going or we stopped the game since no end condition was near to be met. With all those hidden objectives the game has almost no direction and in the end you may be lucky to score a 2nd place in an objective only known to one of your opponents. I would rate the gameplay 7 or even higher, but the ending and scoring always falls flat for me.

RATING: 3
Take that diplomacy type game disguised as civilization / exploration. 

AP prone should avoid. Hidden victory conditions don't make it a better game. Resources and money can go out of circulation before a game end mechanism has not been met. Game rewards informal negotiations and back stabbing both of which are not in the games mechanics. Only a few character cards allow direct actions against other players, so not everyone will have to ability to retaliate, which leaves everyone else at a big disadvantage and at the mercy of the fortunate player. So, there is really no defense and really makes any longer term planning a waste of effort.

Three player is not a very exciting game since all of the victory point rewards have 1st, 2nd, and 3rd and tied place also get points. Four player is almost as bad as three. Seems to play best with five.

Seems too long and too much work for the "fun".Have played four games and they experience got worse with every game - not interested in playing any more. A better choice is Glen Drover Age of Empires or Martin Wallace's Struggle of Empires.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.8
(+) Beautifully produced game (except for the coins which are too small)

(+) Theme nicely integrated in gameplay

(+) Plenty of opportunities for negotiation. Interesting player interaction.

(+) The game often offers interesting decision making for each player individually or for the entire group. This could well be the main feature of this game.

(-) Combination of secret goals with secret end conditions brings a certain level of chaos into the game

(-) Point scoring opportunities are very limited. After playing for a long time victory or defeat could depend on an (unknown) detail.

(-) Player interaction in this semi-cooperative game is somewhat twisted: Players must work together to avoid a scenario in which all lose, but the player who contributes the least to this efford has the best chance of winning. This is actually a problem, because doing a real efford in order to avoid a collective loss will in many cases also cost you your personal victory. In a semi-cooperative game I prefer a game which does a better job in creating win-win opportunities.

(-) The secret end conditions can be a bit unfair: some players will have greater control over when to end the game compared to others. Moreover, these conditions make the game time highly unpredictable which is rather impractical

(-) Some not very elegant rules dealing with trade actions, buildings, employability of workers, ... which makes explaining/understanding the game harder and which makes pauzing a thematic game to search for game rules in the manual a recurring pain

(-) Some ameritrash-elements are mixed up in a game which revolves around euro-style mechanics. For some this is sure to be a feature, but in this game, I take this as a disappointment. There is luck of the draw in goal cards, exploration tiles, demand cards and luck is also induced by some character/technology cards.

Approx game time: unknown. Depends on goal cards. The game offers a different card set if you want to play a long, longer or ultra long game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.8
A mix between worker placement and Clue, where you try to run and exploit a rich and fertile archipelago together with your semi-co players. 

It has loads of different mechanics, and it can be a bit of a pain in the ass to get it all started up, but once you manage that initial hurdle, it runs like a charm and everything interacts with each other. Every mechanic (markets, population happiness and control over population) influences another, and your action can influence them as well.

The real trick of the game is that every player has a different set of end-game conditions, interests and goals which will remain hidden for the entire game. 
Because everybody has different (and in most cases conflicting) win conditions, the game will quickly turn into a game of (broken) promises, backstabbing and exploiting. 

But... (yes, big but!) 
if you only keep your own goals in mind, you'll quickly find out that the indigenous population isn't all too happy with their new exploitive masters, and you'll soon have a rebellion on your hands, which means all players lose the game (except if the rebellion was all according to your insidious plan because staging a rebellion was your win-condition).

If you want to run this game to the end, you and your semi-co players will have to find a balance between exploitation, investment, collaboration, backstabbing and your own interests.

RATING: 1
I am utterly confused about this one. What those who like the game tell me doesn't connect with my experience at all. 

So, maybe it was that the explanation of the game bored the hell out of me. It's a thematic euro with ameritrash level of details and complexity. Which makes the theme more fleshed out, but unfortunately it's one of those themes I couldn't care less about. It's about micro managing your empire/colony/whatever and the level of detail is ridiculous, so if you're that weird niche who like both theme and optimisation games - this is the one to get. 

And then the whole groupthink level which I'm not convinced it works. Ok, it works with some groups, I see it falling apart by other groups (like mine). Ok, so you're focused on your little imperialistic enterprise, trying to gather stuff, to build stuff, to sell stuff, to buy some other stuff and some more stuff. But, somehow your focus must shift and realise this is a co-op. Of sorts. You see, either everybody loses or everybody wins, but somebody wins more than the others. Got that? There's more: somehow an idea that if everybody loses there's a chance somebody wins regardless (one of the hidden goals is helping the native uprising) is supposed to make people work together??? 

Anyhow my game went like this: 1 hour of explanation, 30 minutes of game where nobody helped anybody and my goals appeared unreachable and my situation dire, so I said screw you people, I'm not saving your asses. And we all lost the very next turn. The main problem was that with all the micromanaging and empire building, the group I played with completely ignored the idea of working together and bypassed the notion of "winning together". 

So my observation is: this needs a specific group of people who are either so competitive or so friendly or a bit of both in order to make the game work. For me while this seems like an interesting idea, the accounting core of the game holds no interest for me and I'm more than happy to throw spanner in the works and finish the game early. Also the solipsistic level of gaming is for me at ods with the cooperative vibe (or even semi-cooperative vibe). The rating does not reflect quality of the game, just my level of interest. And overburdened overambitious design, doesn't help it.  I'd be interested to see a game with this dynamic and 1/20 of the rules. And a more unique theme.

RATING: 3
[2013-10-12]
Féltem ettől a fél-koop játéktól és úgy tűnik nem véletlenül. Már a Red November sem tetszett amiatt, hogy van olyan eset, hogy mindenki veszít mégis nyerhet egy valaki. Sajnos ebben a játékban is van ilyen, sőt egyértelműen a legkönnyebb az ezt a célkártyát húzó játékos dolga. Sokat elmond a játékról, hogy sokan enélkül a célkártya nélkül játsszák a játékot. Minden játékos kap egy titkos célt, illetve van egy mindenki számára látható közös is. Ez teljesen jó. Felfedezők vagyunk az új kontinensen és kezdetben közösen küzdünk a helyi őslakosság fellázadása ellen. 6 féle nyersanyagot termelünk, amit az ottani vagy európai piacon tudunk értékesíteni. Felfedezünk, különböző épületeket emelünk és kártyákat vásárolunk, ami adott dolgokban ad előnyöket. Nem tetszett a felfedezés szerencsefaktora. Ha nem tud az ember új hexát lerakni, akkor veszett az akciója, semmi kompenzációja nincs. Érdekes a feljövő kártyák szerencsefaktora is. Összesen 5-6 kártya lehet az 50 körüliből, ami pontot adhat a játék végén. Mivel kevés pont van ezek a kártyák rettentő értékesek lehetnek és sokszor a szerencsén múlik ki veheti meg őket. A kártyák ármechanizmusa viszont teljesen jó ötlet, forgatásukkal változik az áruk. Tetszett a piacműködés, de az persze egyáltalán nem új ötlet. A célkártyák végfeltételei is elég kiegyenlítetlenek, van amire nagyon kicsi az esély, van amit akár egy játékos is jó eséllyel próbálhat befolyásolni. Beleraktak licitáló részt is a játékba. A legtöbbet licitáló dönti el az összes játékos sorrendjét az adott fordulóban. Tehát elképzelhető, hogy a második legtöbbet beadó játékot 5. lesz egy ötfős partiban. Ezen elég sok minden múlhat. A játéknak azonnal vége, ha teljesül egy végefeltétel, nem kell egy teljes körnek lemennie, így előfordulhat, hogy 1-2 játékos nem ugyanannyi kört játszik, igazságtalan szerintem. Érdekes játék lett az Archipelago. Mindent össze akartak gyúrni, nagyon a témaközpontúságra mentek, de ennek a játékmenet látja kárát. Nekem az alapkoncepció fél-kooperatív jellege sem tetszik, mert véleményem szerint ritkán lehet esély arra, hogy mindenkinek érdeke legyen a továbbjáték és ne a mindenki vesszen felé húzzon valaki, ha nem jól áll. A szerencsefaktorok is a téma miatt kerültek bele, de nem biztos, hogy ez jót tesz a játéknak. Úgy érzem sok kisebb nagyobb hibát követtek el a készítők, pedig lehetett volna ebből egy nagyon jó játék is. Így sajnos nekem átlag alatti lett.

RATING: 9
It has been a very long time since I have been this excited about a Euro style game.

There are too many elements to this game that should not have made it out of the Euro clean rooms - too many little boxes on the checklist of perceived negatives that were missed. This game somehow escaped its Euro upbringing with its soul intact.

Nearly every element is Euro to be sure, but it wants to do its own thing with those elements.

There seems to be a whole bunch of variants and suggestions proposed to "fix" this game. People feel the need to herd this beast back to their comfort zones where everything is safe and sound.

Quit it.

There are already, oh, about a zillion other perfectly comfortable Euros to choose from. No need to bring in the sanitizers and scrub away the Scary Things. Had this game been safe and comfortable, I wouldn't have given it a second thought. 

The game is beautiful. The exploration aspect is fun and all the parts come together to create a very different game with each playing.

I really appreciate the way the partial/hidden victory points are implemented, as well as the game ending conditions. If you are having trouble with this aspect, approach it like this (and teach it like this):

1 You always know two victory objectives - the evolution card (known to all) and your personal secret objective. Work toward these points.

2 Stay balanced. If you build a little of everything, instead of just focusing on one or two objectives, you have a better shot at making it into more scoring positions.

3 Stay flexible and aware. Figure out what others are doing. React quickly. Why did she build a second port? She didn’t really need a second - Oh.

This is all easier said than done, but this type of gameplay is a blast. It keeps you engaged, and the process is fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
1 play, 4er

Wow, this is a pretty cool game! It's a mix of exploration, worker placement and area majority (sort of) with public and secret objectives and negotiation. It may turn out that there's too much negotiation for me, but I still look forward to playing this one again!

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
For setup, players will create a map of hexes and place their starting tokens (boats, meeples). Hexes show different layouts of land and water; water shows some number of fish icons, while land shows x (0-5) resource icons (several types in the game) and 5-x huts. As they're placed, advance 1 space/hut on the Surplus Worker track. The domestic and export market boards are also placed in display, with several goods seeded to the domestic market. Another board tracks the total number of meeples on the map. Five Evolution Cards are placed on display (rest are in face up deck). Players each get a secret objective card, while one is put on display. The secret cards each have a game-end condition, 

Each round:
1. Untap all your used cards and reset all meeples.
2. Blind bid for turn order; winner sets everyone's order. Tied players rebid (if tied again, no one wins). ALL bids are spent.
3. Resolve boards 1-4, which may result in moving the rebellion or Surplus Worker markers.
4. Balance of the Archipelago: resolve the event/crisis on top of the Evolution deck. 
- top half = domestic crisis; lay all meeples down, then in turn order players spend resources to stand up meeples at ratio on card (may freely take from domestic market if desired); any meeples still down add one each to rebellion marker
- bottom half = export crisis; in turn order, pay resources from export market or personal supply; advance rebellion marker once per missing resource
5. Actions. On your turn, in any order:
- use a worker to take an action (harvest resources, build/use buildings, move meeples, explore, breed, hire worker, tax = earn 1 coin/worker, or conduct 1 transaction in domestic or export market)
... explore: take top tile of stack, or discard it and use the next one; they're double-sided, and are placed such that one of your tokens could get there; if successful, get an explorer token (which are in stacks; everyone gets extra workers when 1st and 2nd stacks are depleted)
... move: move 1 hex, although may move over chains of boats
... harvest: move your available meeples to icons on their hexes, all of one type; collect 1 cube each
... build: each has a different cost, and is placed in hex; place meeple building it on top. Buildings (4 types) allow access to markets, or stand up all meeples on tile during rebellion, or give you control of all buildings and resource icons on the hex)
... breed: every pair creates a third; max 3 meeples/player/hex (token-limited)
... hire: take workers from trained natives board, reducing count as you do so
- use an Evolution card you've previously purchased
- pay 1 coin to use a market or port building (to conduct 2 transactions at domestic or export markets)
6. Evolution Cards. In turn order, buy a card and rotate another, or rotate 2 cards. The card prices change with each rotation, until they are discarded. At end of turn, replenish display; if red zone revealed on card at top of deck, resolve it immediately. 

During actions:
- if rebellion marker ever exceeds population marker, everyone loses (unless someone's secret card is the Traitor)!
- if any player's secret end-game condition is met, they must announce it; game ends at end of current player's turn. Then, everyone reveals their objective cards and everyone scores points for them and for the public objective. Also score VP for any Wonders (some Evolution cards) built. The player with the most VP wins!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
I want so bad to love this game, but there are some huge barriers preventing that.

There's so much to love with this game.  The design of the exploration mechanic is well-done and entertaining, there's variety in the "special power" cards offered up each turn, there's depth in the worker placement wheel and it's interaction with the game map, hidden goals (a favorite mechanic of mine) that scores everyone a la Troyes, and the resource economy is balanced and well-implemented in the entire game.  I even like the unemployment/savage mechanic and how it's implemented into the game.

What I don't like are the consequences and effects of that mechanic.  Mainly that if the number of savages gets too high the game is over for all players and how much and how often players control the number of savages.  Many of the actions raise the unemployment/savage numbers and each turn one or more crises happen that players need to give up resources to resolve.  The problem with this "semi-cooperative" mechanic is that players who have resigned to losing (or just plain assholes) can end the game for everyone out of spite.  Exacerbating this is the fact that one of the hidden goals wins the game for only that player if this happens.

If the revolt only caused a global punishment (not global game loss) this game might be one of my favorites.  I still actually really like it in spite of its weaknesses but I just can't love it, especially due to the amount of time and effort this game requires be invested that can just become completely unraveled.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Sprawling semi-cooperative civilization (?) game chock full of resource gathering, exploration and so on. What makes it exciting is that various factors raise the number of unhappy natives; and if the number of rebels gets too high, all players are executed and lose. If the crisis is kept at bay long enough, players win and a "grand winner" is chosen.

My one-sentence elevator pitch is, I think, "Traders of Genoa with consequences." It's a negotiation game. Players have their own engine-building and their own targets for points, but the threat of disaster is constantly looming. Negotiation isn't limited to the crises, of course -- perhaps you want to pay to harvest resources in a territory another player controls, or trade for mutual benefit -- but that's where it comes to the fore. 

The rebellion is too much for any one player to avert alone, but do you want to give up [i]your[/i] resources for the good of all? How much will you pay to get someone else to contribute?

It's spectacular and chaotic, a rollercoaster of unpredictable and uncontrollable crises. Maybe with experience I will be able to deduce other players' scoring cards, maybe not, and I'm not sure I will ever be comfortable with the lack of control, especially given the possible length of the game. But it sure is an interesting experience: a glorious, intriguing mess.

RATING: 5
This game is very difficult for me to rate... while I do find the game enjoyable and there are a lot of interesting decisions and mechanisms involved... the game feels like it is just fundamentally flawed.

The whole "build up but not too much or you all lose" just goes against everything that the game presents. You want to do well and expand and build a lot, but if you do it pushes the natives to too fast to handle and kills you. Too many people have to "bite the bullet" so everyone doesn't lose. This is even more compounded if a player gets the traitor card and just constantly taxes and goes for a native win. Ultimately the person who decides not to "bite the bullet" the most will win.

Also, the game is fairly random in what exploration tiles you get in relation to what the VP cards are. The VP cards feel most balanced around a long game with very experienced players, but you'd have to play this game dozens and dozens of times to get to that level.

So difficult to rate a game like this. There's a lot to like but a few ideas that just destroy it all. Feels like it would be better as a full co-op where everyone scores VP equal to the lowest player so everyone is working together. Not sure if there is really an easy fix or if the game is just flawed to the core.

RATING: N/A


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Truly outstanding semi-cooperative exploration and civilisation game. Players take the roll of colonising powers on an imaginary island group and have a wealth of actions available to them each turn. These include exploring new parts of the archipelago (thereby increasing the size of the modular game board), harvesting resources, selling to local and foreign markets, constructing buildings, and many more. The cooperative aspect comes into play as players have to maintain a 'balance' to the area by keeping the natives happy through avoiding over-taxation and not introducing too much foreign labour, and being able to supply enough resources to both types of market. Each player has their own secret end of game objective for scoring, as well as an end of game condition, which is a clever aspect of the gameplay as it means that no-one is quite sure when the game is likely to end and what other players are attempting to do to score points (this can range from anything from having the most towns, owning the most explore tokens, having the most progress cards etc) and all players benefit from them. This is such an ambitious game and it succeeds on a number of levels, but primarily on account of it being a deeply strategic experience that incorporates a wealth of Euro-style mechanisms yet maintains a highly thematic feel. I hope that this begins a trend for similar heavy Euros where the theme is integral to the overall design of the game.

RATING: 4.8
The more I think about this, the less I like it. The action selection is fairly generic but worked well and seemed fresher because of the circular board. The discovery seemed to massively favour those who found stone in the early turns and then those who found tiles that fitted later on.

I really am starting to dislike hidden objectives, especially when scores might be so low that happening to have achieved an extra card was with 1/3 of someone's score.

Oddly though despite all of these issues, I quite enjoyed my first play.

Update: On a second play, the game felt less chaotic and it was largely possible to formulate a plan and stick to it. That said, the traitor card is bloody stupid and so if they are last in turn order the game encourages losing players to abandon the game and let everyone lose as resources are so tight that they have no incentive to spend anything to keep the game going.

Further update: The more I think about this, the more convinced I am that this game is broken as it comes. In my most recent play of it, I offered the player who was able to avert the crisis that killed us enough resources to effect a four point swing in his favour. This would have taken him from last to joint second. This is before he collected stone which was being offered by another player and was a known VP condition. However, because the resources I was offering were hidden, neither he nor I knew their true value. How can you negotiate with resources when their relative values are unknown and largely unknowable? To fix this game, you probably need to start with more money, have all objective cards face up and completely change/abandon the turn order bidding. Of course, you'd still have a lot of useless, overpriced cards in the game.

Rating down from 6.4 to 4.8, but this could easily be my first one rating. I think, in most circumstances, it is broken.

RATING: 7
This game is gorgeous and busy on the table. I dig the modularity: its design evokes a rogue-like, and I love rogue-likes.

I've played two three-player short games, with new folks both times. Neither game was "short;" you'll spend an hour teaching the rules, and then 2.5 hours hazily recalling them across five rounds.

Archipelago creates a weird brainspace: it is uncertain, overcomplicated, and strangely tense. Eeeverything you do tips the see-saw, but you can't really tell how. It's bizarrely happenstance under the guise of being nuanced and intricate. I hate the hidden objectives. I still want to like the game.

The solo scenarios are okay, but they don't entirely work: the crises and the economy break too arbitrarily without multiple players to bolster them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a great game. Very nice mechanics, extreme variety, clever victory conditions. It is a fascinating game because you do not know when the game will end, you try to deduct the victory conditions.
But the original game is cooperative and it does not work (the player in last place always will force the rebellion).
We play with the following changes:
Benefactor variant : benefactor is always in place plus one more victory condition, and the pacifist and the rebellion cards are always out of the game.
In the turn order phase, only the winner pays money (everyone else gets his money back (in a case of a tie; all tied players pay money and they bid again; if nobody bids, turn order remains unchanged). This gives penalty to the player that wins the order auction (going first is too powerful in this game).
The game with the above changes becomes fully competitive and it shines!

RATING: 7.5

Really, the ONLY complaint is that the game can be extremely susceptible to luck. This is particularly true when playing a solo scenario, as starting conditions and resource options at hand can be very bleak for many of the character choices.

Supply and demand, and their impacts on the workforce, both domestic and export markets have never been modeled so well in a board game before!

RATING: 9
Absolutely awesome. Archipelago combines about 6 different games into one delicious broth, and although I only get it to the table around once or twice a year it is so worth it.

The components are absolutely gorgeous, the adjustable game length works fantastically well, and the semi-cooperative nature of the game leads to some VERY interesting situations. There is a tenuous balance between your individual economic plans and the larger-scale dilemmas the game throws at the group. Maybe one of you is a gross saboteur though, and wants to see it all come crashing down. Finding the sweet spot between being competitive and being realistic is always so engrossing. 

If you can get the appropriate group together (and if you can find any copies) I can't recommend it highly enough. 

(Although the subject matter is something to consider before purchasing. A game about colonisation and exploitation will not suit everyone, and the game's depiction of marginalised groups definitely leaves something to be desired.)

RATING: 6
This feels like two games - a complex yet elegant exploration, resource and resource control game with a dense, possibly unnecessarily complex overlay with the kitchen sink (special cards, workers vs. rebels, secret objectives that determine most of the game VPs, and even a secret traitor!).  

It really doesn't help that the game has beautiful but nearly unreadable iconography, or that it's critically important to read exactly what special cards are in front of every opponent, and this is really difficult to do because of a lack of any iconography at all on many of these cards.

I'd want to try this again with a subset of the rules, the parts that I think might work just fine without all the bells and whistles.

RATING: 8
Archipelago was also one of my "first" modern board games, and learning the rules was a serious undertaking.
Teaching the game to my wife and other friends was another challenge in of itself, as there are constantly things that need to be considered throughout each phase of each turn.
Archipelago is full of challenges - the frequent "crisis" and "balance" events/phases in the game are often a turnoff for players, especially once the action starts to heat up and the play area has grown substantially from the initial tile.  Also, the "evolution card" phase of the game really drags the pace of the game down, as players have gone from hashing out moves on the playfield to stopping and reading a bunch of cards, dealing with crisis events as new cards are played, and dealing with the resulting depletion of resources.  Also, as stated above, it's typically good to have at least one player who has studied the rules significantly, who can be the one to chime in and say things like, "No you can't explore there as your colonist in the neighboring tile is engaged" or, "No you can't place that worker/offspring there - you already have three colonists on that tile" or, "no you can't perform that action due to the restriction caused by this other player's active evolution card..."  Of course, those corrections often frustrate the other players, and makes the knowledgeable player seem like a "know-it-all."
All that said however, the game can really be a lot of fun once all the players really start to understand the strategy, and even more so when players start to work together to keep the game balanced and working smoothly.  I truly appreciate that its a game you have to work together in order to keep playing, but can still have your own, hidden agendas.
