RATING: 8
Archipelago is an incredibly bright and happy looking game. As you explore and build the island chain, it continues to foster generous thoughts and co-operative gameplay. But then an economic crisis happens. The people demand cattle for food and clothing. The island produces plenty of cattle, yet the market is empty. Who is hoarding the cattle?!? The indigenous population grows restless. They are now refusing to work. The crown demands ore, but the workforce refuses to mine it. The crackdowns come and portions of the island start to fall to insurrection. You start to suspect one of your fellow colonists has gone native. He has decided to throw off the yoke of tyranny!

At least now you know where all the cows went.

I love this game.

That's not to say it doesn't have a few issues. One, the rulebook isn't very clear. When exactly a worker can move or not move when working a building is confusing. There is also an issue where less savory players can grief the game and send it spiraling out of control. The resources are delicately balanced between demand and availability. While this is necessary to create the tension that occurs when honest players are trying to scrape off a little selfish profit, it flat out doesn't work when someone foolishly tanks a market out of spite.

I don't play with that sort, so the game works beautifully. It is a very ambitious design and a completely unique take on the worker placement/ resource swapping genre. I do wish it was a little more intuitive and the procedural side of maintaining the various markets holds down my rating just a bit, but I could eventually even get over that, because the game is so much fun.

RATING: 10
Archipelago is a excellent mixture of Role Playing and Euro games. It gives you a Euro scenario, with Euro mechanics but it lets you be whatever you want.

You don't need to draw a "do bad stuff" card to screw your friends. Also, you don't need to build armies to protect yourself from the jerk who's screwing everyone. Everything in Archipelago can be done and solved with the greatest game mechanic of all time: talking.

Every kind of negotiation is allowed in Archipelago, you can trade basically anything anytime you want, you may bribe someone to just stop doing whatever he is doing, or you can just convince someone to do something else because you have reasonable arguments. I mean, Archipelago is real life in a board, you have to deal with the player's personalities and be smarter in all levels.

Very deep game, I love it!

10 games / 5 Plays

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Strange game. What makes Archipelago so hard to classify, for me, is that all the ingredients for a great game are basically there. The components are great, the visual design is wonderful, the flavor and feel of the game are very much present in gameplay, the mechanics are relatively elegant, you get a bucketload of options without feeling overwhelmed, the duration is customizable (depending on playgroup and available time)... One would think this has to be a great game. 

Unfortunately, it's not. It's not bad, not by a long shot, but it feels somehow... frustrating. Mainly because you get the feeling so much time went into designing balanced mechanics and beautiful bits and bobs that one fundamental question got overlooked: what am I playing for? Sure, the hidden objectives are nifty, but feel unsatisfying. It basically boils down to spending a couple of hours doing all sorts of interesting things, only for the game to suddenly end, after which you count stuff and determine who won, usually by a very slim margin. The problem with this is that it feels as though there's a disconnect between the actions you've been taking all throughout the game and the scoring. Sure, you work towards your own objective, and you can deduce what certain other players are up to, but literally anything can be taken into account in the scoring and there's always something you overlook. Archipelago plays as though rather than taking a core victory mechanic (i.e: this is how you win) and building a game around it, the game designers instead started by designing a great thematic balanced game with all sorts of interesting mechanics, only to realize they forgot to put in a victory condition at the very last staff meeting before release. And so the victory conditions ended up being stuff that was already in the game. It's all stuff you do anyway - buildings you build, resources you accumulate... you just need to have one more than your neighbors. 

The fact that the level of immersion is immense only worsens this feeling. When you make a game this epic, you want an epic conclusion, like, I dunno, world domination. Or something. As it is, only the rebellion feels like a glorious ending, and that's the one you want to avoid. Everything else is just mucking about to grab a few points left and right.

RATING: 7.5
I liked it after one play. It feels like the anno series on pc. A civilization game were you balance population, unhappiness, employment etc together as a group. I see a lot of potential play and lots of room for negotiation.

After more plays (without traitor): 
I really like the mechanisms in this game but not so much the hidden objectives. This is a game were I enjoy playing it more than winning it. Sessions tend to be very long even if there isn't that much negotiation going on. The handling of the little crises during evolution phase are annoying because they abruptly interrupt the gameflow and cause downtime. 

I have not played with traitor since I played it with different people and didn't want them to become frustrated immediately, but the more I play it, the more I recognize the traitor is an essential mechanism.

RATING: 7
Wow. Group dug this hardcore. I was totally surprised. We all agreed that the goals are a bit too random to be strategic enough for consistent plays, but this game will definitely hit the table once in awhile. 

Edit: 9/28/15

RATING: 7.5
BEAUTIFUL game that feels way more adversarial than you might first think. Several innovative parts, and I love the exploration of the islands and building up the land. Variable game length options are available, but none are ludicrous in length.

And props to the art on the hexes too. You'd be hard pushed to find a nicer looking game.
